"},{"id":33838,"link":"https:\/\/www.nba.com\/news\/qa-patrick-beverley","date":"2019-05-11","title":"Q&A: Patrick Beverley on close friend Derrick Rose and his first free agency","content":" Near the top of the unwritten list of players who opponents hate to face but would love as a teammate resides a relentless, 6-foot-1 \u201cdog\u201d named Patrick Beverley. He\u2019s the defensive-minded point guard who made a name for himself years ago with the Houston Rockets, bringing indefatigable energy and a fearless mentality against the game\u2019s biggest stars while earning stripes as a professional nuisance in the process. Each of those qualities transferred with him to the LA Clippers \u2014 the franchise Beverley was traded to in a multi-player deal for Chris Paul two summers ago \u2014 and were on full-blown display in their most recent playoff appearance against Golden State. Beverley, who was assigned the unenviable task of guarding Warriors star Kevin Durant, brought the intensity to a series many experts thought would end in a sweep. Helping get Durant ejected in Game 1, he then hounded the two-time Finals MVP in Game 2 as Durant was held to eight points and attempted just three shots when matched up against Beverley , his primary defender. The game ended with LA staging the greatest comeback in NBA playoff history. While the Warriors would win the series in six, and Durant would respond with the prolific performances he\u2019s accustomed to, Beverley has undoubtedly earned the respect of his peers. He sat down to discuss that, free agency, his favorite teammate ever and his relationship with fellow Chicago native Derrick Rose. NBA.com: You\u2019ve really earned the respect from people across the league. LeBron James recently complimented you and a couple of other teammates. He called you a \u201cdog\u201d on the court. What did you think of that comment and what makes you such a dog out there? Beverley: I just go out there and try to play the right way. I think my biggest trait is to play extremely hard within the rules of the game. It\u2019s just different. When you\u2019re in L.A., you have a different platform than playing anywhere else. Brighter lights. Bigger media. I\u2019ve always been that type of dog player. But coming from Houston then coming to L.A., and of course playing Golden State on the biggest stage, it\u2019s easier to notice more. NBA.com: Yeah, you guys didn\u2019t get too many prime-time games this year. Beverley: We only got like three (national) games this season. So yeah, a lot of people couldn\u2019t see it. NBA.com: Some spectators have labeled you as an \u2018irritant\u2019 on the court. Do you embrace that word as well? Beverley: Nah, I just go out there and be me. I\u2019m the same way if I play cards, if I play video games, if I\u2019m competitive with the homies, I\u2019m the same way. If people choose to have emotions or feelings behind the way I am all the time, then that\u2019s on them. NBA.com: Was Game 2 against the Warriors the craziest game you\u2019ve ever been a part of? Down 31 in the third, and making the biggest comeback in NBA playoff history. Beverley: Honestly, we were so lost in the game that we didn\u2019t even know we were down that much until we looked up and it was a close game and we thought we had a chance. We just wanted to get lost in the game and that\u2019s what we did Game 2. NBA.com: Talk about your relationship with Derrick Rose. Two guys from Chicago, played against each other in high school and are somewhat best friends. Beverley: He\u2019s like my brother. Anything I ask for, he gives it. Vice versa. Whether it\u2019s a car to drive if I\u2019m in his town for a week, or if his girl flies to L.A. and (the Minnesota Timberwolves) haven\u2019t gotten there yet, then she comes to my house until they land. It\u2019s just a family thing. We always support each other. Always have each other\u2019s back. If someone\u2019s going through surgery, we\u2019re right there with him. It\u2019s that Chicago love.                    NBA.com: Did you watch the emotional documentary that came out this year of him finding out he got traded from the Bulls? Beverley: I didn\u2019t even watch. I don\u2019t like to watch all that stuff. NBA.com: Why not? Beverley: Because I understand the type of player he is. And the way he puts his heart into things. You don\u2019t want to see something like that. NBA.com: What were your thoughts on that trade, going from you guys\u2019 hometown to the city of New York? Beverley: I thought it was a good opportunity for him. I thought he was leaving another platform and starting over new. Away from home. Finally get a chance to be his own man and start fresh. Besides the injuries, he played well in New York,. He came back and played well in Minnesota. If he\u2019s healthy, he\u2019s gonna be a dog. NBA.com: Do you think he still has a case to make the Hall of Fame if he keeps at it? Beverley: I think so. I think he\u2019s gotta put a little bit more work in. I know his goal is trying to get Sixth Man, but I always tell him \u201cjust go out there and be you, dog. Whatever that is.\u201d https:\/\/www.instagram.com\/p\/Bp5k8puBdpH\/ NBA.com: You\u2019ll be a free agent this summer for the first time. What will help determine Pat\u2019s approach in free agency? Beverley: I feel like I can play with any team. Whatever team that is, I feel like I can make an instant impact. It\u2019s all about doing the right things. Building a new culture. Whether it\u2019s a young team that I\u2019m with and I have to build a new culture, or if it\u2019s a team that\u2019s already established and I have to figure out how can I fit in and make this team better, I\u2019m selfless when it comes to that. NBA.com: Some people have linked you to Dallas. And then you sent out that tweet to Luka Doncic. What was that about? Beverley: I saw him follow me (on social media) earlier. So I\u2019m thinking the same thing. I\u2019m just having fun with it, man. It\u2019s a good time to be a free agent. NBA.com: Could you see yourself having fun playing alongside a player like Luka? Beverley: Of course. He\u2019s ball dominant, makes the right plays. If that does come, it would be me, Luka, Tim Hardaway Jr., (Kristaps) Porzingis, Courtney Lee. It\u2019s tough when you actually think about it. But I\u2019m going to have fun with free agency man. I work hard. I\u2019m gonna enjoy this process. NBA.com: If you were to change teams, you\u2019re bound to make an impact on the culture as you stated. You were a part of the rise of the Rockets and now these reinvigorated Clippers. What goes into changing the culture? Beverley: I go out there and be me. I call a spade a spade. If you\u2019re doing things the wrong way, I\u2019m going to let you know regardless if you\u2019re the max-contract guy on the team or the two-way guy on the team. I\u2019m gonna keep it real consistent. I\u2019m real black and white, not a lot of grey with me. You gone get all of it. NBA.com: You had sort of a \u201cblack and white\u201d exchange with your opponent Paul George this season. You told him \u201cI locked you up.\u201d Can you talk about that situation and locking star players up? Beverley: I just locked him up that game. I was fortunate they came off a back-to-back. I look at all that. I don\u2019t think he thought that (the Clippers\u2019 coaching staff) was gonna put me on him. Kind of threw him off guard. I won that battle that night. I won that battle. Fortunate for me, it was the last time we played them in the season, so I know he\u2019s gone come out next year ready. But yeah, I felt like that game I locked him up. And I did. NBA.com: Speaking of Paul George, he called that epic shot by Damian Lillard a bad shot. Lillard called it bad defense. Where do you stand? Beverley: Man, Dame\u2019s been shooting them shots since he\u2019s been in the NBA. And all the shots Paul George shoots are tough shots anyways, so it\u2019s funny that he argues about bad shots when he makes all the toughest shots that players can\u2019t make. Him and KD. NBA.com: You were on that Rockets team when Lillard hit that other series-ending buzzer-beater a few years ago. When you saw the one he made against OKC were you like, \u201cwow, D\u00e9j\u00e0 vu\u201d? Beverley: That\u2019s the same thing I said. (I remember being) on the court, watching the ball go through the net. NBA.com: It\u2019s a lot of up-and-coming players in the league. You played with two nice rookies in Shai Gilgeous-Alexander and Landry Shamet. What other young player should people look out for? Beverley: Jerome Robinson. We drafted him right after Shai. He played in the playoffs and he played well. I think he\u2019s got a chance to become a point guard.  NBA.com: Were there any young players you guarded in games and thought \u201cman, he might be a problem in the future?\u201d Beverley: Trae Young. I say Trae Young and Luka. They\u2019re both tough. NBA.com: What is something you wished the common fan understood more about basketball? Beverley: The IQ of it. People see all the dunks, all the 3s and all of that. And in people\u2019s eyes, if you\u2019re not LeBron James, Kevin Durant, Stephen Curry or the unicorns, or the superstars on your team, they think every other NBA player is not talented, and that\u2019s the biggest myth in the world. It takes role players to allow the Kevin Durants and superstars to be them. Take Draymond Green (for example). You take him off Golden State, does the ball move? It might. Do they win? They might. We don\u2019t know, but with Draymond Green on that team, he brings a toughness. You have to respect it. A lot of fans don\u2019t really see that and how big the role players are in the NBA. NBA.com: I\u2019m an Atlanta guy, and I\u2019ve always enjoyed watching Atlanta native Lou Williams. I think he\u2019s one of the most underrated players in the league. Beverley: Yeah, Lou\u2019s like a brother to me. He puts in the work. He kills on the court. I feel like he should be getting so much more credit. I feel like he should be in the All-Star game, especially these past couple years. NBA.com: You two seemed to have developed a great bond. Has he been your favorite teammate so far in the NBA? Beverley: Favorite teammate ever. Ever. Favorite teammate ever. When I came to Atlanta, he was the first person I got up with. We talk about everything, family. He\u2019s like my brother. We\u2019ve spent a lot of time together with the Rockets and Clippers. That\u2019s gonna be a big point this offseason. If I do choose to leave (the Clippers), I\u2019d be leaving him, my brother Lou Will. And that would be tough on me. NBA.com: What do you listen to in the pregame to get you ready? Beverley: Future. What has mattered in one game hasn\u2019t mattered in the next. Each team has twice looked dominant, and two games have gone down to the wire. The closest thing to a constant has been the importance of Joel Embiid\u2019s presence on the floor. Never was it more important than in the Sixers\u2019 112-101 victory in Game 6 on Thursday, when Philly outscored Toronto 100-60 in Embiid\u2019s 35 minutes and 33 seconds of playing time and sent the series back to Toronto for a deciding Game 7. Embiid was healthier than he was after suffering through an illness that limited his effectiveness in Games 4 and 5. But his standard box score stats \u2013 17 points, 12 rebounds and two blocks \u2013 were relatively unspectacular. But his plus-40, the highest mark of the postseason , tells the story. \u201cIt\u2019s a huge plus-minus number in a playoff game,\u201d said Sixers coach Brett Brown. \u201cHis significance is felt all over the place.\u201d Single-game plus-minus isn\u2019t always an indicator of a player\u2019s importance. Sometimes, he\u2019s just on the floor when a game swings in a certain direction. But a game-high plus-minus is nothing new for Embiid, who\u2019s now a plus-133 for the playoffs, with no other Sixer better than a plus-94. Game 6 was a game of runs, which had a lot to do with whether or not Embiid was on the floor. After Greg Monroe registered a minus-25 in 16 total minutes over the Sixers\u2019 losses in Games 4 and 5, Brown went to Boban Marjanovic as his backup center in Game 6. Marjanovic, who had only seen garbage-time minutes since registering a minus-22 in less than 12 total minutes over Games 1 and 2, checked in for Embiid with the Sixers up three and 6:58 left in the first quarter. The Raptors then went on a 5-0 run, with all five points being scored at the rim or at the free throw line. The lead was gone and so was Marjanovic, with Embiid checking back in with just two minutes and 23 seconds having elapsed off the clock. And over the final 4:35 of the period, the Sixers outscored the Raptors, 16-6. At the end of the first quarter, Embiid hadn\u2019t scored a point, but he was a plus-13 in a game his team led by eight. \u201cHis presence,\u201d said Raptors coach Nick Nurse, \u201cwas a lot more than his baskets tonight.\u201d The Sixers went up by 19 early in the second quarter. But with the lead at 17, Marjanovic checked back in for Embiid at the 8:09 mark. And this time, he didn\u2019t even last as long as he did in the first quarter. The Raptors scored on five of their next six possessions (a stretch that included two dunks and a layup), Brown called a timeout, and Embiid was back on the floor with just two minutes and 10 seconds having elapsed. \u201cThat was a hard four minutes for Boban,\u201d Brown said. \u201cI felt like we pivoted out of it quickly.\u201d Marjanovic didn\u2019t play again until garbage time. Mike Scott was the backup center in the second half, but that didn\u2019t help much. With the Raptors going on another quick, 5-0 run (starting with a dunk from Kawhi Leonard), Embiid\u2019s first third-quarter stint on the bench lasted only a minute and two seconds. Through six games, the Sixers have outscored the Raptors by 21 points per 100 possessions in Embiid\u2019s 192 minutes on the floor and have been outscored by 46 points per 100 possessions in his 96 minutes off the floor. Embiid\u2019s impact is obvious. On offense, he\u2019s been drawing double-teams, setting impactful screens, and drawing loose-ball fouls on the glass. \u201cYou can see it on pick-and-rolls,\u201d Embiid said. \u201cMarc is not helping off of me. They\u2019re really stuck to me and every time I put it on on the floor or every time I have the ball, they just collapse.\u201d On defense, he has essentially scared the Raptors from scoring near the basket. With Embiid on the floor in the series, the Raptors have shot 59 percent in the restricted area, where they\u2019ve taken 23 percent of their shots. With Embiid off the floor, those numbers rise to 80 percent and 28 percent , respectively. \u201cYou\u2019re conscious of where he\u2019s at on the floor,\u201d Kyle Lowry said. \u201cWhen he\u2019s out there, he\u2019s a big body. He\u2019s a force on both ends.\u201d Still, the on-off swings in Game 6 were rather remarkable. There\u2019s certainly some noise in that plus-40 mark. The Raptors\u2019 bigs got some open looks from the outside on Thursday, but Marc Gasol and Serge Ibaka combined to shoot 0-for-6 from beyond the arc. If a couple of those go in, this story might be about Jimmy Butler and Ben Simmons, who combined for 46 points, 14 assists and just two turnovers. Butler capped off a 19-point first half with two buckets and a strip of Leonard in the final 11 seconds. Simmons, meanwhile, registered nine buckets in the restricted area (one shy of his career high), thanks to a relentless attack and four offensive rebounds. As they head back to Toronto for Game 7, the Sixers still have to figure out what to do when Embiid goes to the bench. \u201cWho is the eighth man?\u201d Brown asked. \u201cIt\u2019s not clear.\u201d It may be that there is no eighth man, that Brown just extends the minutes of his best players, and he tries to buy a few minutes with Scott at center. Brown certainly didn\u2019t hesitate to bring Embiid back quicker than usual with the Sixers\u2019 season on the line in Game 6, and Embiid says he\u2019s ready for whatever\u2019s needed on Sunday. \u201cIt\u2019s the playoffs,\u201d Embiid said. \u201cI got to play. If I got to play 45 minutes and push myself out there, that\u2019s what I got to do. If that\u2019s what it takes to win, if my presence on the court is needed, I got to be there. It doesn\u2019t matter if it takes the whole game. I\u2019m fine with it. I got to keep on pushing myself. In Game 7, we\u2019re going to need it. I\u2019m going to need to be on the court and I intend to be ready for everything if I have to play a whole game.\u201d We don\u2019t know what\u2019s coming in Sunday\u2019s Game 7 ( 7 ET, TNT ). But Embiid will be on the floor, and that may be the most important thing. * * * John Schuhmann is a senior stats analyst for NBA.com. Steve Kerr won\u2019t have Kevin Durant, who \u2014 in Kerr\u2019s widely-shared opinion \u2014 \u201chas been the best player in the NBA in these playoffs\u201d for the rest of this series. Yet with Durant ailing with a strained calf muscle , the Warriors haven\u2019t been stripped completely bare on the floor or, just as important, on their fingers, which are decorated with bling and the credibility that goes with it. \u201cWe know the challenge ahead is a big one,\u201d Kerr said. \u201cBut they\u2019re champions and I think there\u2019s an advantage to having championship experience. This group will never be doubted.\u201d The Warriors bring a 3-2 series lead, the comfort of knowing a potential Game 7 would be in Oakland, and a proven core led by Stephen Curry. Still, beating the Rockets without Durant is the steepest roadblock in this Kerr dynasty. He\u2019s that good of a player, obviously, and the Rockets with James Harden are that much of a threat. That said, the Warriors faced a pair of elimination games three years ago against Durant and his erstwhile Oklahoma City teammates. Then Klay Thompson went scorched earth with 41 points in Game 6 of the Western Conference finals. That effort was followed by a collective Golden State showing in the clincher a few nights later. And the Warriors did trail the Rockets 3-2 last season , but Chris Paul missed the final two games with a hamstring pull and Golden State seized the opportunity . Still, the task at hand represents a bind for the two-time defending champions. Durant has served as a security blanket throughout these playoffs, while Curry and Thompson have struggled with their shot in this series , especially from 3-point range. Whenever the Warriors needed bailing out, Durant usually came through with smashing results. Now, that high-level insurance will be missing from this series and perhaps a portion of the Western Conference finals (if the Warriors advance). The MRI on Durant\u2019s calf showed a strain and he didn\u2019t travel to Houston. He had a similar injury last season and missed nearly two weeks. All this makes for a throwback challenge for the Warriors, to see if they can muster the same amount of production at both ends as they did before Durant arrived. Those results earned them two trips to the NBA Finals, one championship and a record-setting 73-win season . \u201cHe\u2019s had (this injury) before and he\u2019s responded well,\u201d Kerr said. \u201cWe\u2019re disappointed he won\u2019t be able to play in this series, but if we can win the series and move on it will look good for his return in the near future.\u201d Kerr is citing the past in order to rouse his team\u2019s confidence, although the present doesn\u2019t exactly equal the past. For starters, Andre Iguodala, Shaun Livingston and Andrew Bogut were all younger and friskier. Of the three, only Iguodala is flourishing , and only to a degree. With Durant out, the Warriors will lean on Iguodala to play more minutes and be more active in looking for his shot. Livingston\u2019s role in the rotation is diminished and Bogut is virtually out of it. Speaking of the bench, it\u2019s weaker than before, when the Warriors had Marreese Speights, Leandro Barbosa and others. That means Kerr will press his role players for more \u2014 can they deliver, especially in such a heightened situation? \u201cWe\u2019ll just find somebody on the bench who can give us 35 points, two blocks and 10 boards,\u201d Kerr wisecracked. \u201cObviously it\u2019s a huge loss, but our team has a lot of confidence. We trust each other. We\u2019ve won championships together, so we come out and give it our best shot. \u201cWe\u2019ll try to mix and match some lineups and find contributions where we haven\u2019t had them in this series. Guys who haven\u2019t had the opportunity will get it. Yes, it\u2019ll be a little different, but no reason why we can\u2019t go get a win. It\u2019s about finding the right rotations and combinations and getting enough minutes for our core guys to get some rest.\u201d For certain, the Warriors can\u2019t afford any of the mainstays \u2014 Curry, Thompson, Draymond Green \u2014 to falter. They\u2019ll be asked to play in the 40-minute range and must avoid foul trouble while fighting against fatigue. Because it\u2019s an area of the game where physically, certainly, there\u2019s no benefit to being a man, as opposed to a woman, when it comes to refereeing,\u201d Silver said. \u201cThe goal is: Going forward, it should be roughly 50-50 of new officials entering in the league,\u201d he said. \u201cSame for coaches, by the way. The Houston Rockets managed to win anyway to go up 3-2, but he was finished and the Rockets dropped the next two games and the series to the Golden State Warriors. In Game 5 of the Western Conference semifinals last night, Kevin Durant grabbed his right calf in Game 5 and limped off . The Warriors managed to win anyway, 104-99, to go up 3-2 , but he is \u2026 well, let\u2019s see where he and this series goes from here. Once again, a non-contact injury to a significant player casts a spell on Rockets vs. Warriors, throwing everything for a loop. It\u2019s all about the recovery powers of Durant, whose right calf strain was placed on the clock 48 hours before a looming Game 6 in Houston, with an MRI pending before the team plane leaves Oakland. Update: Durant to miss remainder of semifinals after MRI confirms calf strain He wore a limp and an empty expression after the game, obviously concerned and certainly confused. He was hoping he didn\u2019t play his final game of this series or, worst case scenario, in a Warriors\u2019 uniform (Durant will be a free agent this summer). Sure, this isn\u2019t as ominous as Paul\u2019s hamstring which instantly shut him down for the summer . He missed three games last year with the same injury, although the severity won\u2019t necessarily be identical. For the Warriors, this is a hope-for-the-best, prepare-for-the-worst situation. \u201cWe\u2019re going to have to find a way,\u201d said coach Steve Kerr. \u201cHe\u2019s been on this incredible playoff run. Hopefully we can do enough to win one more game in this series. That\u2019s the plan.\u201d As painful as it was for Durant when he winced after drilling a 3-pointer, the real ache was experienced by the Rockets, who blew a chance to win and close out the series at home. Tied after three quarters and no Durant, the elements that the Rockets showed in the previous two games, both wins, turned to vapor. There was no grit, no furious 3-point response and curiously no James Harden, who shockingly took just three shots in the quarter for a quiet five points and never seized the moment. PJ Tucker stepped on the line while hitting a 3-pointer. Paul had his shot blocked by Kevon Looney. And Harden chose to defer when, up until this point in this series, his foot stayed on the accelerator. If the Rockets lose this series, they will rue the fourth quarter where they didn\u2019t attack or reach for the jugular of a Warriors\u2019 team that played at a major disadvantage. \u201cI don\u2019t think we relaxed,\u201d Paul said. \u201cIt was just a few big plays. You know what I mean?\u201d It was one of the biggest quarters of the Kerr era, considering the circumstances. They asked Stephen Curry to bail them out and he did so, looking not quite as deadly as he was prior to Durant\u2019s arrival in 2016 when he carried the Warriors, but good enough. \u201cI think you saw Steph go into a different mode when Kevin went out,\u201d Kerr said. \u201cHe knew things were going to run through him. He was brilliant when we absolutely needed him.\u201d Curry had 12 of his 25 points in the fourth quarter , with Klay Thompson assisting with seven. Durant replacement Looney was uplifting, getting sneaky with a key offensive rebound (which he passed to Thompson for a 3-pointer), blocked shot and steal in the closing moments. Even Jonas Jerebko hit a critical 3-pointer. The Warriors somehow managed to pull it off \u2014 thank you very much, Rockets \u2014 but now the intrigue begins. \u201cIf Kevin is out, then what you saw in the fourth quarter is what you\u2019re going to have to see going forward,\u201d Kerr said. They\u2019ll certainly lean on Curry, who won a pair of Kia MVPs prior to Durant and is one of the greatest shooters in history, although you wonder if his knees might buckle. Curry hasn\u2019t lifted his game all postseason and has lapsed in and out of a case of the shooting yips. Even in the early moments Wednesday, he whiffed on three layups and didn\u2019t score his first basket until the second quarter. For his career, Curry\u2019s impact in the postseason hasn\u2019t matched his regular season impact. In this series, Curry is shooting 26.3 percent on 3-pointers , abysmal for him. But because Durant has been scorching \u2014 averaging 33.2 points per game against the Rockets \u2014 Curry\u2019s miscues have only been mildly irritating. \u201cWe trust the man,\u201d Thompson said. \u201cEverybody in this world trusts him with the ball in his hands. His track record has proven it. He\u2019s coming up in the clutch.\u201d Additionally, Thompson can\u2019t afford the dry spells that plagued his first four games of this series. Thompson managed to break out of the fog with a strong first half Wednesday, disappeared in the third before rising again in the fourth. The Warriors will need more than just Curry to compensate for Durant\u2019s scoring. \u201cIt\u2019s frustrating when you don\u2019t play up to your standards,\u201d Thompson said. \u201cI\u2019m never going to waver with my confidence when it comes to shooting. That\u2019s what got me to this point. Just want to be aggressive from the jump and make the right play. It\u2019s that simple.\u201d The pre-Durant Warriors won two titles, 73 games in a season and reached three NBA Finals. But this isn\u2019t pre-2017. Andre Iguodala\u2019s age is up and production is slightly down. Andrew Bogut is on the bench. And speaking of which, this bench is weaker (with no David West or Leandro Barbosa). Understand that the tightness of this series places an urgency on both teams to produce at a high level, or else. There hasn\u2019t been a blowout win by either team and Game 5 was decided much like the previous four \u2014 by three or four plays that swung the outcome. \u201cThis series has been a battle,\u201d Thompson said. \u201cEach game, such little margin for error. I know (the Rockets) aren\u2019t going to feel sorry for us, or the media or the fans. It will be gut check time. We go down there and give it our best effort. If we do that, I have confidence every man on this team will step up in his absence.\u201d When Durant signed as a free agent with the Warriors in the summer of 2016, it caused ripples of annoyance throughout the league. Here was an NBA champion that came a game from winning another title and then added an MVP in his prime. The jealousy and fear was not unfounded as the Warriors have led a charmed life ever since. They ripped through two more titles and lost only one Finals game with Durant, who won back-to-back Finals MVPs. But if they must proceed the rest of this series without Durant, compassion isn\u2019t something they can expect. Because for the Warriors, who bring the \u201cSplash Brothers,\u201d Draymond Green and plenty of championship experience, they know the deal. They know what Durant, once seen as an added luxury, means to their mix. \u201cWe\u2019re not the same team without him,\u201d Thompson said. * * * Shaun Powell has covered the NBA for more than 25 years. "},{"id":33893,"link":"https:\/\/www.nba.com\/news\/bucks-advance-celtics-exit-with-questions","date":"2019-05-09","title":"Lots to blame, plenty to credit in Bucks' mastery of Celtics","content":" MILWAUKEE \u2013 One man\u2019s trash, another man\u2019s treasure. This guy\u2019s ceiling, that guy\u2019s floor. One team\u2019s giddy success, the other team\u2019s dismal failure. When a playoff series ends as swiftly and decisively as the Milwaukee Bucks\u2019 ouster of the Boston Celtics from the Eastern Conference semifinals ,  there\u2019s a rush to assign credit and blame. And yes, it\u2019s a zero sum game. So there is going to be math on this test. How much of what transpired in the Bucks\u2019 4-1 series victory in the best-of-seven series was a testament to their performance and overall season, which will continue next week against either Toronto or Philadelphia ? How much was it about the Celtics shrinking in the moment, snuffing a largely unsatisfying year with the most bitter aftertaste possible? Is 51\/49 a copout as an assessment of liability? That\u2019s the breakdown that springs to mind, at least this mind, with the Bucks getting the extra couple points as a nod to the positives of winning. There was much to praise, and nearly as much to bury. Let\u2019s start with the disappointing yin and get to the effervescent yang later. Boston brought so much baggage into this season, it\u2019s hard to send the Celtics off into summer without dwelling on the gaps between what was supposed to be and what was. No, they didn\u2019t make good as the team most likely to replace Cleveland atop the East and as the conference\u2019s Finals rep. No, the return of Kyrie Irving and Gordon Hayward \u2013 max-contract players missing from the postseason festivities last spring \u2013 didn\u2019t make a difference. Unless the difference was downward. Boston went from Game 7 in the conference finals last spring to just five in the semifinals this time. The Celtics showcased a somnambulant regular season as if they were a veteran contender pacing themselves until the money games began. Except that, that\u2019s not what they were. They were a young team, a team that underwent significant changes to its lineup and rotation, a team that needed each and every one of those 82 lab sessions to develop chemistry and assorted intangibles. It didn\u2019t help that speculation about New Orleans star Anthony Davis\u2019 eventual whereabouts got stirred into a pot of Boston rumors with its own players\u2019 futures. Point guard Kyrie Irving, in particular, might have played his last game in a Celtics uniform, finally arriving at the great uncertainty that hung his entire season. After another dreadful shooting performance Wednesday at Fiserv Forum \u2013 6-for-21, boosting Irving to 25-of-84 in Boston\u2019s four consecutive defeats \u2013 Irving was as distant and glum as if he\u2019d just learned his contract option was the Celtics\u2019, not his. \u201cYou take your lessons and the ass-whooping they handed us and you move on,\u201d Irving said. \u201cFor the rest of my career, I\u2019ll never forget something like this. The taste of feeling defeat and this type of style, I haven\u2019t felt [losing in five games]. For me, it\u2019s just moving on to the next thing and seeing where it ends up.\u201d Ya think? Irving has had one foot in, one foot out the door all season. That not only stymies the hopes of President of Basketball Operations Danny Ainge, who, by all accounts, wishes to team Irving with Davis, but it also dragged a cloud over what they all might have accomplished in the here and now. Even bigger than the one Irving generated in this year\u2019s Bucks series by playing in it. \u201cThere\u2019s no question,\u201d Celtics coach Brad Stevens said when asked if the distractions did, in fact, distract his players. \u201cThey have TVs, they have phones. They hear everything. When you live in it, you do your best to kind of black out the noise and focus on what you can control. But these guys are also young men. There\u2019s a lot of pressure to live up to all these expectations.\u201d Stevens vowed his team will learn from all the issues with which it struggled, such as taking 93 shots to score 91 points (the Celtics shot 29 of 93, missing 32 of their 39 from the arc). And repeatedly getting shredded by not just the big names in Milwaukee\u2019s starting lineup, but also by the role players off its bench. Reserves such as George Hill, Pat Connaughton and Ersan Ilyasova contributed so admirably in the series, coach Mike Budenholzer had some trouble finding guys who qualified for the garbage minutes. Whereas Boston\u2019s offseason began immediately, Milwaukee\u2019s impressive postseason rolls on. The Celtics were co-conspirators in their demise, but Milwaukee\u2019s defense and depth exploited every flaw in and failing of its foe. Irving groused that the Bucks constantly made body contact to mess with his game. Stevens lauded \u2013 and sounded very covetous of \u2013 their habits that \u201cshowed up over and over again.\u201d Said the Celtics\u2019 coach: \u201cWe make a teaching tape every year for our teams about different things \u2013 close-outs, recovering, flying around \u2013 and the Bucks are going to be on a lot of clips.\u201d Stevens, a darling among some in the NBA media who has dodged criticism in the opinion of others, didn\u2019t let himself off the hook for the belated sweep. In addition to stating that the Bucks are better than his team, Stevens added: \u201cI did a bad job. As a coach, if your team doesn\u2019t find your best fit together, that\u2019s on you.\u201d By the end of 240 minutes spread across 10 days, the critical difference between  the two teams was this: Boston has a talented roster, while Milwaukee has a team. There\u2019s a pecking order, a togetherness, a selflessness, all the things that showed themselves only for as long as the first-round series against Indiana and Game 1 against the Bucks lasted. The habits to which the Celtics reverted from Game 2 and beyond were at the far end of the scale from Milwaukee\u2019s. Budenholzer spoke at length before tipoff about his team\u2019s personality. It\u2019s easy for teams that win to have great chemistry, but it\u2019s special when the bonds within a team generate the success. \u201cThe chemistry that this group has has been cooking in the oven since the summer,\u201d Budenholzer said. \u201cGetting together for non-mandatory type workouts, different places working with coaches, working together through the month of September and open-gym environments and preseason. So if you put it in that context, all of that was happening before there was a single win.\u201d Unlike Boston, too, Milwaukee was deadly serious about the regular season. Its most notable achievement \u2013 only once losing two games in a row \u2013 became a driving factor that paid off after the loss that opened this series. \u201cThe most important thing about this team is after Game 1, I got a text from this guy \u2013 [Eric] Bledsoe \u2013 saying that, \u2018We\u2019ve got these guys,\u2019\u201d Giannis Antetokounmpo shared. \u201cAnd that\u2019s what happened. Once your teammates are all on the same page \u2013 you have their back, they have your back \u2013 things like this are gonna happen.\u201d * * * Steve Aschburner has written about the NBA since 1980. With a win tonight, the franchise would advance to its first conference finals series since 2001."},{"id":33888,"link":"https:\/\/www.nba.com\/news\/john-lucas-houston-rockets-qa","date":"2019-05-08","title":"Q&A: John Lucas brings wealth of experience to Rockets","content":" * Tonight On TNT: Rockets vs. Warriors, Game 5 (10:30 ET) The first pure point guard ever taken with the No. 1 overall pick in the NBA Draft was John Lucas out of Maryland in 1976. That was an era that valued pass-first players at the point and Lucas defined such a role, although the game was by far the domain of the big man and size was paramount. When the Rockets made Lucas the choice, legendary Celtics boss Red Auerbach exclaimed: \u201cA point guard No. 1? What\u2019s this league coming to?\u201d Lucas\u2019 stint in Houston would initially be short and later repeated twice more, as he fell prey to substance abuse, became a journeyman and saw his career constantly interrupted. Although he played 14 years total , he became more known for flunking two drug tests and being released in March of 1986, the year the Rockets reached the NBA Finals. Lucas was averaging 15.5 points, 8.8 assists and 1.2 steals as a starter in what was perhaps his finest season before the plug was pulled. Rather than become a statistic, Lucas salvaged what was left of his career; at age 33 he averaged 17.5 points for the Bucks. After retirement, he devoted his life to helping other athletes overcome abuse and addiction or other issues: Ty Lawson, Tyrann Matthieu, the late Roy Tarpley and Mitchell Wiggins, the father of Andrew Wiggins, among others. He made Houston his base for that, and became a mentor for talented yet troubled souls. Eventually, he formed a company that also helped train young players for college and professional ball a made a name for himself in Houston basketball circles. Lucas also had three stints as a coach, first with the US Basketball League\u2019s Miami Tropics (where a number of his players were former substance abusers) and then the Spurs, Sixers and Cavaliers. His timing was poor in Philly and Cleveland; he was fired before the Sixers drafted Allen Iverson and by the Cavs before they drafted LeBron James. Lucas is now a Rockets assistant coach in charge of player development and has the unique position of having ties with both Houston and the Warriors in this second-round series; Lucas played for Golden State in the late \u201870s. He sat for a recent interview and discussed his sobriety, his stint as a professional doubles player in tennis \u2014 Lucas was a singles champion in the Atlantic Coast Conference while also playing basketball \u2014 and when he coached the Spurs and dealt with the country\u2019s tabloid couple at the time, Dennis Rodman and Madonna: * * * Shaun Powell, NBA.com : You\u2019ve had a front-row seat for the last three years to see James Harden. What do you know about his game that others might not? John Lucas, Houston Rockets coach : He\u2019s got the fastest feet I\u2019ve ever seen for someone who scores as much as he does. I\u2019m not talking about footrace speed. Just basketball speed and movement. It aids his step-back jumper. I\u2019ve worked with Kobe and LeBron and his feet are faster than anyone I\u2019ve ever worked with. And he uses his body well. His size is perfect for his game. SP : You were just 6-2 and 175 in your day. Could you guard Harden, and what advice would you give for anyone trying to guard Harden now? JL : I would just holler `Help!\u2019 And that would be my advice for anyone else. Get help. Lots of help. SP : As a former point guard, what do you appreciate most about Chris Paul?  JL : Chris is the one whose game reminds me of the way I played, although I wasn\u2019t as good as he is. He\u2019s going to beat you with his brain. He\u2019s athletic but not the most athletic. He is the most cerebral. He\u2019s going to find out where you are and he has a knack for knowing what the other team\u2019s going to do. Some of the things I\u2019ve learned from him that I\u2019ve passed onto young point guards is him knowing how to stay a step ahead of the other player. Chris is always one step ahead of you. SP : But center Clint Capela gives you the most pride, correct? JL : He\u2019s had to travel a longer road than James or Chris, and he\u2019s come a long way from when I first got here. We\u2019ve worked together and I saw the growth. It\u2019s all because of his work ethic and his desire. That made him into the player he is today. SP : Do you still remember draft day, 1976? JL : The draft was done on the phone. There was no TV show with hosts and commercial breaks. You just got a call. That\u2019s it. And the whole thing took maybe an hour. They didn\u2019t take point guards that high back then, but then came Magic (Johnson) and Allen Iverson and a few others after me. There\u2019s two positions you gotta have, point guard and the center. Even with the analytics, that hasn\u2019t changed. SP : You were in denial about your substance abuse troubles; you were suspended by the Warriors after missing six games and showing up late for practices in 1981, but that wasn\u2019t rock bottom. When was that? JL : I missed a game March 14, 1986. I was with the Rockets. I woke up on the street and didn\u2019t know what happened. The game was in Portland and I didn\u2019t make the trip. I had a $3 million contract on the line but only if I could stay sober for the season. We went to the NBA Finals that year but I wasn\u2019t around for it. SP : What was the reaction from the Rockets? JL : Bill Fitch was the coach and he said, `We\u2019re out of the rehab business.\u2019 Well, he saved my life. I tell him that all the time. I love him for that. SP : And you never looked back? JL : I was sick and tired of being sick and tired. They say you never recover until you hit rock bottom and I didn\u2019t know how to help myself until I hit rock bottom. SP : This obviously made you qualified to help start and serve as an advisor for the NBA\u2019s drug program, and also help others, right? JL : I tell the people I help that you can\u2019t fool somebody who has been everywhere you ever thought about going. I always thought `denial\u2019 was a river in Africa. I\u2019ve lived it. I\u2019ve gone through it. Only 1 out of 10 of us make it back, make it through recovery. A lot of them save their lives but lost their career. Some that have had both gone. You don\u2019t want to be where I\u2019ve been. I\u2019ve seen the devil. SP : And you\u2019ve never looked back. How\u2019s life in recovery? JL : Every day is a new start in your life. What you did yesterday doesn\u2019t define who you are. That\u2019s who you used to be. That\u2019s not who you are. SP : We\u2019ve seen a number of successful athletes have issues, when seemingly they have it all. Why is that? JL : Being a star is probably the most depressed world you can live in. Because now you don\u2019t know who\u2019s going to be there as your friend and who loves you as a person. SP : While you were still playing in the NBA, you dabbled in pro tennis in the World Team Tennis league and was the mixed doubles partner of Dr. Renee Richards, the transexual player who entered the US Open as a woman in 1977. Explain that experience. JL : For my recovery from drugs and alcohol, I had to accept people for who they are. I just wanted to be loved because I\u2019d screwed up so bad, and so people from same sex marriages and someone like Renee, I could identify with, because like them, who are often rejected by society, I wanted to be loved. I wanted someone to accept me again. We were great as partners and got along well. Tough to beat us on the court, too. Both of us lefties. Big serves. We went something like 28-1. A great time. SP : You coached the Spurs right before they hired Gregg Popovich to be the team general manager, and right after Larry Brown left. You had David Robinson, a straight arrow, but also Dennis Rodman on that team. And he had a special guest, right?  JL : He was going out with Madonna at that time. I brought her in the locker room. I introduced her to the guys. But I didn\u2019t know it would become such a distraction, all the attention she brought from the media. It really hurt us in the playoffs because it distracted us against Utah in the first round. Dennis was ejected from one game and suspended from another game, which we lost at home. It was our year to win it and we lost in the first round. SP : But at least Madonna was on her best behavior, I suspect? JL : Very nice, very smart woman, down to earth. Very businesslike. She was trying to help Dennis to help become more businesslike. SP : What in your past has helped you today as a coach? JL : I\u2019ve been the No. 1 guy and the last guy and the aging veteran, so I know all parts of the game. I deal with players in all of those phases. Sometimes a player might be upset about his role or playing time. I try to walk through their process of being in the rotation. I understand the feeling. SP : How far can the Rockets go this season? JL : All the way. We have an MVP and Chris Paul. Mike D\u2019Antoni is fun to play for because he lets the players get the best out of their abilities. This is a good team, a great team. * * * Shaun Powell has covered the NBA for more than 25 years. You can e-mail him  here , find his archive here  and follow him on  Twitter  . "},{"id":33886,"link":"https:\/\/www.nba.com\/news\/nuggets-separate-themselves-game-5-rout","date":"2019-05-08","title":"Nuggets put Blazers on the ropes with series-shifting Game 5 rout","content":" DENVER \u2014 Two points separated the Denver Nuggets and Portland Trail Blazers after the first four, grueling games of these Western Conference semifinals . They piled up the same number of 3-pointers and free throws as well. The games were that good, that tight, and the difference between the two teams was negligible at best. Then Tuesday night happened. Paul Millsap happened. Nikola Jokic happened. Jamal Murray happened. The manifestation of a Nuggets team that\u2019s been dancing with a destiny that leads to the Western Conference finals, finally happened. Their 124-98 rout of the Trail Blazers in Game 5 at Pepsi Center was the sort of declaration Nuggets coach Michael Malone has been predicting for his team since they were locked into a back-and-forth struggle with the San Antonio Spurs in the first round. They evened the series Sunday in Portland, showing mettle beyond their years by snapping the Trail Blazers\u2019 12-game home winning streak that dated back to the regular season, with an inspired effort to stave off the certain doom of a 3-1 deficit. Tuesday night\u2019s salvo was a seismic shift in the opposite direction. The Nuggets\u2019 biggest lead was 31 points and their intentions were plain for everyone to see. Millsap roasted the Blazers for 24 points and eight rebounds, dominating while being featured more and executing his considerable advantage in small-ball situations. \u201cThe best thing about Paul Millsap is he\u2019s true to himself, he never tries to be something he\u2019s not,\u201d Malone said. \u201cHe\u2019s not a rah-rah guy, he\u2019s not a guy that\u2019s going to be screaming and yelling. But I think his calm demeanor has an effect on our group. Young team going through all of this for the first time and when you can look to a four-time All-Star with 90 playoff games under his belt, that\u2019s reassuring. He\u2019s kind of the calm for our team and I think that has a tremendous impact on all of our young players.\u201d Two in particular during this postseason and this series, to be sure. Jokic led the way with 25 points, 19 rebounds and six assists before fouling out late, leaving little doubt as to who deserves to wear the crown as the best big man in the league right now. Murray was splendid again, with 18 points and nine assists, while his backcourt mate Gary Harris chipped in with 16 points and six rebounds. Will Barton and Malik Beasley scored 10 points each off the bench, leading a 33-point bench scoring effort that will need to travel back to Portland for Thursday\u2019s Game 6 if the Nuggets have any chance of winning three straight and ending this series in six games. \u201cWe know going to Portland for Game 6 is going to be really tough,\u201d Malone said, referencing his team\u2019s Game 6 struggles in the first round. \u201cGame 6 in San Antonio, we did not come ready to play, mentally or physically. I hope that we have a much different mindset going in to Portland for Game 6.\u201d The Blazers have some serious tweaking to do, in a short amount of time, as well. Their starters didn\u2019t even play in the fourth quarter, Terry Stotts acknowledging that the 30-point hole his team was fighting out of might have been too large, given the circumstances. And the need to preserve the energy of Damian Lillard, CJ McCollum and the crew for what sets up as their biggest game of the season was obvious. \u201cAt this point, it\u2019s one game at a time facing elimination,\u201d Lillard said. \u201cWe know that we\u2019re more than capable of getting it done in the next game. We don\u2019t feel like we\u2019ve played our best basketball yet, and with our back against the wall, we don\u2019t really have a choice. Our mindset is to just get to the next one, take care of home and make it back here.\u201d Stotts has adjustments to make before Thursday night as well, after the Nuggets bludgeoned his team in the paint for a 66-44 scoring advantage, while also outrebounding them 62-44. The decision to switch Enes Kanter\u2019s primary defensive assignment from Jokic to Millsap Tuesday, with Al-Farouq Aminu being tasked to try and contain the much bigger Jokic, backfired as Millsap went to work immediately on Kanter. \u201cThey just played harder than us,\u201d Kanter said. \u201cI think that was probably \u2026 even the coach said, probably this was our worst basketball the last six weeks. Shots didn\u2019t fall in, on defense we weren\u2019t really communicating with each other, we didn\u2019t really trust each other. We\u2019ve just got to learn from this and just go home and take care of home, because right now, that\u2019s the most important game of the year.\u201d The atmosphere Thursday night at Moda Center promises to be electric. The Blazers have long enjoyed one of the best home atmospheres in the league. But will it serve as the advantage it has in the past when the Nuggets are fresh off two straight huge wins in this series, the first on that floor? \u201cWe have two must-wins,\u201d Stotts said. \u201cSomebody was going to have a must-win after tonight and it\u2019s us. So we have two must-wins ahead of us.\u201d That four-overtime loss in Game 3 Friday night could have been the emotional breaking point for the Nuggets. It wasn\u2019t. A school shooting Tuesday morning in a Denver suburb where Malone lives with his wife and daughters rattled the coach and an entire community. That sort of life-altering event could easily have sidetracked Malone and his team. They persevered. The Nuggets were locked in from the start. When it became clear that the Blazers weren\u2019t going to be able to keep up the pace, they kept pushing until the final buzzer. They understand the opportunity staring them in the face; a conference finals date with the two-time reigning champion Golden State Warriors or Houston Rockets, who are tied 2-2 heading into Game 5 Wednesday (10:30 ET, TNT) at Oracle Arena. His 68 points over Games 2 and 3 weren\u2019t enough, and the Raptors needed every bit of his 39 to win Game 4 in Philadelphia on Sunday and essentially keep their season alive. The guy was averaging 38 points on 62 percent shooting, and they were a possession or two from being down 3-1. Embiid couldn\u2019t put the ball on the floor without losing control of it, and he didn\u2019t have the energy to get in the post, too often settling for 3-point attempts that the Raptors were happy to have him shoot. Some of that improved offensive rhythm was a product of what has been the No. 1-ranked defense in the postseason. There was no wondering if the Sixers were just too big for VanVleet or if Gasol needed to be more aggressive. With the series tied 2-2, the Bulls needed to win Game 5 to advance in the best-of-five series. The pivotal game was tight throughout, with six lead changes in the final minutes of regulation. Cavaliers guard Craig Ehlo gave his squad the lead 100-99 with 3.0 seconds left. The Rockets won, 112-108 . Yes, this is now closer than identical twins and possibly headed to the same finish of a year ago, when the Warriors took out Houston in seven games during the conference finals. And if you look under the hood and examine the parts, you\u2019ll see that in the moments of truth over the last two games that Houston won and created this deadlock. This doesn\u2019t mean they\u2019ll be the better team at the finish line, whether in six games or seven. But right now, they\u2019ve made this contest closer than most imagined and given themselves a shot in what is now a best-two-out-of-three. \u201cIt\u2019s a dogfight, and every possession matters,\u201d said Chris Paul. Tucker is just 6-foot-6, yet brings the temperament of a honey badger in a bad mood when it comes to grabbing rebounds. For the second straight game, Tucker snagged double-figures and been especially menacing on the offensive end; he\u2019s going for seconds and sometimes thirds in heavy traffic and giving Houston additional chances at buckets. It\u2019s not just ordinary rebounds he\u2019s getting, but the most important ones. That hunger has a psychological effect as well, breaking the spirits of the Warriors while rousting the passion in his teammates. One of these teams is going to beat the other four times. But it may just be a case of survival, one team scratching out four wins and moving on to the conference finals. Through four games, this has been the least efficient series in the conference semifinals, with the teams combining to score just 104 points per 100 possessions. The two teams rank sixth and eighth in offensive efficiency among the eight teams playing in this round. \u201cWe haven\u2019t been in great rhythm here in the last few games,\u201d Raptors coach Nick Nurse admitted Tuesday. \u201cThat\u2019s playing the same team over and over.\u201d The Raptors did score 101 points on 93 possessions in Game 4, their second-best offensive game of the series. The Sixers have been the better offensive team overall, but have really had just one good offensive game and three rough ones. Game 2 was one of two in these playoffs where a team won while scoring less than a point per possession . This series has also been played at a pace (95.4 possessions per team per 48 minutes) slower than that of any NBA team in the regular season. And that could an issue for the Sixers, who played at a faster pace than the Raptors in the regular season and at a much faster pace than Toronto in the first round. The 95.4 rating is only 0.3 possessions per 48 minutes slower than the Toronto-Orlando series, but it\u2019s 10.2 possessions per 48 slower than the Philly-Brooklyn series. Prior to Game 4, it was noted that the Raptors weren\u2019t scoring in transition as efficiently as they did in the regular season. With J.J. Redick having shot 5-for-6 from 3-point range in the first six seconds of the shot clock, the Sixers have actually scored very efficiently in transition in this series: 1.33 points per transition possession. But they haven\u2019t gotten the same number of transition opportunities as they did in the regular season or in the first round. In the regular season, Philly scored 1.07 points per possession ( a bottom-10 rate ) on 20.0 transition possessions per game. In the first round against Brooklyn, the Sixers scored just 1.00 points per possession on 19.0 transition possessions per game . In this series, they\u2019ve scored those 1.33 points per possession, but on just 13.5 transition possessions per game. Against Brooklyn, Ben Simmons averaged 3.6 shots per game in the first six seconds of the shot clock, according to Second Spectrum tracking. In the conference semis, he has just five total field-goal attempts in the first six seconds. The Raptors have seemingly won the pace battle, in part because they\u2019ve averaged only 5.5 live-ball turnovers per game, with Brooklyn having averaged 7.8 in the first round. But Sixers coach Brett Brown explained after Game 2 that, with his team\u2019s shorter rotation and the remaining players playing more minutes, it\u2019s more difficult to push the ball in transition on every opportunity. \u201cWe\u2019ll try from time to time to play as fast as we can,\u201d Brown said, \u201cbut the reality is it\u2019s just a grind. And when you shrink your rotation, you\u2019re probably not going to be able to call upon that type of freshness as much as if you were playing like a normal 9 1\/2, 10 [guys].\u201d The bigger difference between Toronto\u2019s two wins and Philadelphia\u2019s two wins has been on the Toronto end of the floor , where he Raptors have scored 109.4 points per 100 possessions in Games 1 and 4, and just 96.8 in Games 2 and 3. But one number stands out in regard to the Sixers\u2019 offense in wins vs. losses. In their two wins, they\u2019ve shot 45-for-77 (58 percent) in the paint . In their two losses, they\u2019ve shot 37-for-79 (47 percent) in the paint . \u201cWe have to own some of it,\u201d Brown said Monday of his team\u2019s inability to finish in Game 4. \u201cYou give credit to Toronto\u2019s length and their attention to that area.\u201d Indeed, the Raptors played bigger in Game 4 and Serge Ibaka blocked three shots inside. In the regular season, Philly ranked fifth in field goal percentage in the paint (57.4 percent), but 17th in effective field goal percentage on shots from outside the paint (49.3 percent). So defending the former seems like a good priority for Toronto. Of course, if the Raptors are going to collapse in the paint and focus on contesting shots inside, the Sixers will get open looks on the perimeter. They made 12 threes on Sunday , but Tobias Harris (2-for-13) and Mike Scott (0-for-3) were a combined 2-for-16 from beyond the arc. Over the four games, Harris (7-for-22) has attempted more catch-and-shoot 3s than J.J. Redick (9-for-20), which is probably a good thing for the Raptors. Right after Kawhi Leonard hit the biggest shot of the series on Sunday, Harris missed a wide-open corner 3  when the Toronto defense collapsed on a Joel Embiid roll to the rim. If Harris made that shot, it\u2019s back to a one-point game with about 40 seconds left. Alas, he missed and the Raptors made enough free throws to seal the game and even the series. * * * John Schuhmann is a senior stats analyst for NBA.com. Khris Middleton sat down. And the Milwaukee Bucks\u2019 chance of beating the Boston Celtics in Game 4 of their Eastern Conference semifinal series seemed to sit down with them. In a hostile arena, against an opponent that by all rights should have been desperate (though the emotion never did quite translate to the Celtics\u2019 performance), losing your best two players to foul trouble at a crucial point in the second half should have been too much for Milwaukee. Antetokounmpo got whistled for his fourth personal foul with 8:18 left in the third quarter, the teams tied at 59-59. Before the score ever budged, 61 seconds later, Middleton got his fourth. It was automatic for Bucks coach Mike Budenholzer to yank both his All-Stars, with so much game left and the risk of one or both fouling out so great. This should have been the opportunity the Celtics needed. They had misfired their way to that point, shooting 37 percent overall in the first half and 4-of-19 on 3-pointers. But they had their full complement of starters available. Boston should have pounced. Boston should have cracked open the game right there and earned itself a 2-2 series tie. Instead, the Bucks stiffened, then pushed back. They might even have ended the series, turning that stretch of resiliency to end the third quarter into a 113-101 victory . They hold a 3-1 lead now with a chance to close it out at home in Game 5 Wednesday and advance to the conference finals. That\u2019s how pivotal the Bucks\u2019 plucky response to adversity was. They not only fended off the Celtics during that star-starved stretch, they took the lead: Milwaukee went on a little 13-9 run to the 2:31 mark of the third, triggering a timeout by Boston coach Brad Stevens. Then play resumed, and the Bucks outscored them again 8-4 to close the quarter. It was the exact opposite of what should have happened, Milwaukee opening up an 80-72 lead while playing shorthanded, and Boston squandering such a ripe chance to seize the game. Yet there wasn\u2019t much surprise showing in the visitors\u2019 dressing room. \u201cWe were just playing the same way,\u201d said center Brook Lopez. \u201cWe always say, \u2018Same way. Same way,\u2019 and just keep grinding. We did a great job these past two games just grinding for the first 30, 35 minutes or whatever, and then just taking advantage whenever the moment comes.\u201d This should have been Boston\u2019s moment, though. It\u2019s true that the Bucks\u2019 depth has been a weapon all season and that their role players have prided themselves on maintaining \u2014 or adding to \u2014 leads. But c\u2019mon, they were working without a net this time. Antetokounmpo and Middleton had to sit for a while at least, if not the balance of the quarter. The worst thing that could happen if they came back too soon would be picking up their fifth fouls. The second-worst thing would be playing overly cautious to avoid doing that. Didn\u2019t the players who stepped into the breach feel the burden? \u201cWe didn\u2019t really feel that way,\u201d Lopez said. \u201cWe had that trust and belief in one another. We were just trying not to have any sort of letdown.\u201d Budenholzer dealt with the fragile situation by reminding himself that he typically subs out his stars in that general vicinity of the game. Keeping them fresh for the fourth quarter is a priority, particularly with Antetokounmpo. It\u2019s just that this time, the terms were dictated to the Bucks coach. \u201cIt\u2019s always hard to take out Giannis, let\u2019s just start there,\u201d Budenholzer said. But he added, \u201cBecause of our normal subs rotation, it wasn\u2019t as tough to take him out.\u201d Lopez, George Hill, Ersan Ilyasova, Eric Bledsoe, Nikola Mirotic, Pat Connaughton and Sterling Brown all played during Antetokounmpo\u2019s and Middleton\u2019s absences. (Middleton returned for an uneventful final 20 seconds in the period.) Bledsoe got it going offensively, then Hill \u2013 not unlike his super-sub showing in Game 3 \u2013 scored nine of Milwaukee\u2019s final 11 points in the quarter. And they all locked in defensively, making life miserable for a Celtics team that never recovered. \u201cAbsolutely. We\u2019re always defense first,\u201d Lopez said. \u201cI think we even stepped up our intensity in that moment.\u201d The Greek Freak, while all this was going on, sat between deep reserves D.J. Wilson and inactive rookie Donte DiVincenzo with a concerned look on his face and nervous energy bouncing through one leg. Tough benchmate? \u201cI mean, he\u2019s one of those guys who wants to play all 48,\u201d Wilson said. \u201cHe hates when he comes out. He\u2019s kind of like that every game.\u201d Said Antetokounmpo: \u201cIt\u2019s amazing to see that the bench can keep playing hard, keep defending hard and set the tone for us.\u201d The past two games, the Bucks\u2019 bench has outscored Boston\u2019s 74-23. So Milwaukee didn\u2019t just survive, it thrived. It started the fourth with its top guys more rested than usual. And oh, did it show. Antetokounmpo scored 17 points in that quarter, but, playing all 12 minutes during which he scored half of the Bucks\u2019 12 field goals and grabbed seven rebounds. Middleton was scoreless but was a plus-seven the rest of the way, second only to Connaughton\u2019s plus-11. Boston wound up trading baskets for much of the fourth. Al Horford\u2019s layup at 7:25 got his team within 91-86, only to see Lopez and Antetokounmpo score all of the Bucks\u2019 points in a 14-6 stretch that ate up five minutes. He was before the Denver Nuggets came up on the wrong side of a four overtime marathon Friday night against the Portland Trial Blazers, the sort of emotionally draining game that could break a young team. And he was after the Nuggets bounced back 36 hours later and reclaimed home court advantage in their Western Conference semifinal with arguably the most impressive win in a season filled with them. Living through the journey of missing out on a playoff berth last season on the final night of the 2017-18 regular season to earning the No. 2 seed in the Western Conference playoff chase this season, Millsap said he had no choice but to believe. In his teammates, the mission and the resiliency, heart and toughness of a group that has had to learn how to compete at the highest level in real time, given that their catalysts \u2014 Nikola Jokic and Jamal Murray \u2014 had no previous playoff experience. Millsap exuded a quiet confidence that the Nuggets would head home for Game 5 of this series, Tuesday at Pepsi Center ( 10:30 ET, TNT ) , in a good place. Instead of needing a win to stave off elimination, the Nuggets are in a position to press their own advantage \u2014 they were a NBA-best 34-7 at home this season \u2014 with a trip to the conference finals within reach. As the most experienced member of a gritty group, a bunch that\u2019s already been tested by a seven-game grind in its first round series against the San Antonio Spurs, Millsap said he had to lead by example with the season on the line. Murray was spectacular, scoring a game-high 34 points and going 11-for-11 from the free-throw line, including six straight down the stretch to seal the Game 5 win. Jokic was equally spectacular, finishing with his fourth triple-double (21 points, 12 rebounds and 11 assists) of this postseason. Overshadowed but just as critical was the 21 points, 10 rebounds, two blocks and steadying influence Millsap provided. He spoke with NBA.com\u2019  s Sekou Smith about the Nuggets, his journey and more with a monumental Game 5 looming: Sekou Smith, NBA.com : How do you know when you get on a team like this, with so many young pieces still developing, that you might potentially have something special like what you guys have put together this season? Paul Millsap, Denver Nuggets: Personnel. It\u2019s about the personnel. The teammates you have, we have a great group of guys, and anytime you have a great group of guys who are willing to do whatever it takes to win, there is an opportunity there. That\u2019s been there all along, honestly. You could sense it from the start we had after missing out on the playoffs last season. So hopefully, we\u2019ll keep this team together. SS: In your last two stops as a free agent you were a part of a significant turnaround in Atlanta (60-win season and trip to the Eastern Conference finals in 2016) and now this turnaround and run with these young Nuggets. What is it with you and the right timing and the right fit? PM: I value timing. And all of these decisions are based on that and where I was in my career at that time and where I wanted my career to go. So making the right decisions is always important. And then just being the right fit culturally, you know, looking at a team and knowing it\u2019s a team where you can grow, that\u2019s unselfish and willing to do whatever it takes to win, those are all things that have been important to me and things I examined when I was coming up on those free agent situations and trying to make my decisions. I don\u2019t care how long you\u2019ve been in this league, you always have to be growing and improving. That\u2019s critical, your evolution as a professional and as man responsible for your own career. SS: How did you tackle that evolution? You came into this league during a different era, as an undersized power forward known for your rebounding and toughness but have become one of the more underrated two-way players in the league. How difficult was it to dive in and reinvent yourself over these years time and again? PM: It wasn\u2019t too difficult because I was willing to do it. I was willing to put in the time and put in the work. I\u2019m always evolving as a person, so to translate that over to basketball, it wasn\u2019t tough at all. The game has changed, and with the game constantly in that state, you have to change with it accordingly. I\u2019m always the go-with-the-flow sort of person anyway, so with the game moving this way or that way, I\u2019m going to go with it.  SS: As young as this Denver core group was when you joined them before the 2017-18 season, did it appeal to you or was it even obvious to you just how big of a mentorship role you\u2019d signed up for this crew? Because that\u2019s a different burden for a player who is in the midst of the prime of his career, making All-Star games and doing the things you were doing. PM: It\u2019s been somewhat of a challenge for myself, knowing when to and when not to be that guy. Like you said, in the prime of my career I\u2019m still trying to establish myself and reach the goals that I\u2019ve set for myself, knowing that whatever situation you go into there will be some sacrifices that have to be made. I\u2019ve never been a selfish person and I know that if I do the right things as a vet and make sure I help these guys get to where they need to be, it helps me out as well. So I don\u2019t too much worry about myself in that regard, with my game and what I have gong on. Because at the end of the day, production is production and it will always show up. So I don\u2019t get too focused on the numbers or on myself. It\u2019s a team game for me, always has been and always will be. SS: You\u2019ve been paired with other of the league\u2019s best big men before when you and Al Horford were making Eastern Conference All-Star teams together. PM: He\u2019s such a smart player. A lot of things I don\u2019t even have to say. He gets it. I try to help him as much as I can but not to be over the top with it. I try to lead by example every single day at practice. I come in here with a get-better attitude. But with that, with him being so young, he helps me and teaches me as well. Seeing him and watching him, a lot of things that he does and how he studies the game is unbelievable. It works both ways. I\u2019m appreciative of him being on this team and I hope he, as well, of me being the position I\u2019m in. And hopefully, we\u2019ll continue to have great success together. SS: What\u2019s been the key for you in terms of longevity, staying in the league and being as valuable at 34 as you were at 24, if not more? PM: As I said, evolving with the game and taking care of my body. I\u2019ve put a lot of time and effort into my body over the years. And it pays off. I\u2019ve been blessed and fortunate during my journey to meet a lot of people, doctors and therapists, plus with the knowledge I have gained over the years in the areas of health and fitness, I\u2019ve been able to put together a nice team to keep my body healthy and be successful. SS: With that said, what kind of move has Denver been for you in terms of the fit you\u2019ve been off the court?
