Whether it\u2019s moving speeches delivered to his own team or pleading with television audiences to stand up and take notice of the splendid compilation of talent the franchise has stockpiled in recent years, he refuses to let up. From building the legend of Jamal Murray or waxing poetic about the virtues of Nikola Jokic, the nimble giant prone to triple-doubles on the regular, Malone is prepared to use the bully pulpit to make sure no one overlooks the Nuggets. A seven-game series win over San Antonio in the first round produced some of Malone\u2019s best stuff to date, including him trumpeting Jokic as not only a legitimate Kia MVP candidate (true, this season) but also a surefire future Hall of Famer (could be, the way he\u2019s playing). So you had to know Malone was going to be on his Nuggets informercial grind after they refused to lose Sunday in Portland, bouncing back after losing a grueling four-overtime thriller to the Trail Blazers here Friday night with a gritty 116-112 triumph to tie this series at 2-2 headed back to Denver for Tuesday\u2019s critical Game 5. \u201cI\u2019m so proud of our group,\u201d Malone said, after his talented crew showed off the chops needed to regain the homecourt advantage they surrendered in their Game 2 loss at Pepsi Center. \u201cAnd in the closing moments, I really was confident because in close games this year we were 13-3 [in games] decided by three points or less, best record in the NBA. We\u2019re 12-1 in the second nights of back-to-backs, best record in the NBA. Our guys are tough; to come in here and win this game some 36 hours after losing a four-overtime game speaks to just how tough we are. So I wasn\u2019t worried, we had our starting group out there. \u201cJamal, who I thought was phenomenal tonight, goes 11-for-11 from the foul line in a hostile environment and really kind of with the series hanging in the balance. You go down 1-3, and we all know how that story ends. I think the confidence of doing the same thing in the first round against San Antonio helped us, but our guys stepped up. We never frayed. We stayed together. And I can\u2019t speak enough about the resiliency and toughness of our team.\u201d And he shouldn\u2019t. The Blazers had won 12 straight games at home dating back to the regular season and were 22-2 on their home floor since January 5. When the Nuggets saw their 10-point lead shrink to just a point with 3:02 to play as Portland closers Damian Lillard (28 points) and C.J. McCollum (29) led the charge, Denver could have easily folded up under the emotional weight of Game 3 and their current predicament. But they proved to be as resilient and tough as Malone said they were. Jokic was brilliant again, collecting his fourth triple-double (21 points, 12 rebounds and 11 assists) in his first postseason, second only to the five Magic Johnson piled up during his rookie season with the Los Angeles Lakers. And Murray was even better, finishing with a game-high 34 points and draining six straight free throws in the frantic closing seconds to seal the win for a Nuggets team that didn\u2019t allow fatigue, a raucous and sellout Moda Center crowd or the pressure to avoid that 3-1 hole rattle them. \u201cIt wasn\u2019t the first time,\u201d Murray said of his embrace of the pressure with the game on the line at the line. \u201cI think free throws are my thing. My dad and I do a lot of training [on] free throws. Blindfolded, he\u2019ll talk to me just like how the crowd is, put pressure on me. I take 1,000 free throws in practice to make or or two \u2026 and tonight, it ended up being six.\u201d The number Malone focused on afterwards was 11, as in the number of playoff games Murray and Jokic have played in as they continue to establish themselves as postseason stars. \u201cYou think about how young we are and and what we are doing, going on the road and winning a tough game in a hostile environment,\u201d Malone said, \u201cand for Jamal to be the centerpiece of that has been phenomenal. If you\u2019re a Denver Nuggets fan, how excited are you about this team now. More importantly, how excited are you for our future? We have a chance to be a really good team for many, many years and Jamal is going to be a big part of that.\u201d The same goes for Jokic, obviously. He\u2019s already an All-Star and is going to end up on the All-NBA first or second team as well as the top five of the voting for Kia MVP after the regular season he put together. That might explains why the entire Nuggets bench froze as they watched him limp to the sideline in the final moments after being kneed in the leg in the final seconds. \u201cYour heart skips a beat,\u201d Malone said. \u201cNikola is the face of our franchise, but he just got kneed, it was nothing serious and and we were able to hold on for the win.\u201d For all of Malone\u2019s bluster about his group, it\u2019s not even necessary at this stage of the season. The Nuggets earned the No. 2 seed in the Western Conference playoff chase on the strength of a talented and deep roster that might not resonate with casual NBA fans, but is celebrated by those in the know. Touting their accomplishments in real time makes sense for a coach trying to empower his team to believe in themselves in what could and perhaps should be a nice stretch of playoff runs in the future. But anyone paying attention can tell that the future could be now for these Nuggets. A trip to the conference finals one year after they failed to make the postseason field on the final night of the season in what amounted to a play-in game in Minneapolis last April, is a hell of a start. Malone knows it. His team knows it. And so do the Trail Blazers, who are well aware of the opportunity they squandered in a series where wavering confidence by the Nuggets might have been the only advantage they could exploit. \u201cThe good thing for us is that we won a game on their court,\u201d Lillard said. \u201cSo it\u2019s not like we lose both games there. We\u2019re in a good space, 2-2, we know we\u2019re capable of winning on their floor and that\u2019t what we\u2019ve got to get done. Obviously, it\u2019s disappointing \u2026 we didn\u2019t want to let an opportunity like this slip, but it happens. It\u2019s playoff basketball and we\u2019ve got to move forward.\u201d So do the Nuggets, which is where Malone the master motivator comes into play. And just so we\u2019re clear about something, his sell job is genuine. He knows of what he speaks in assessing a young team on the rise, having spent time coaching in Cleveland and Golden State during the formative stages with what would turn out to be teams that made it to The Finals (2007 in Cleveland). He was on Mark Jackson\u2019s Warriors staff when they turned the corner from a lottery team to playoff outfit (2012-13 season), helping nurture the core group of a team that has won three of the past four NBA titles and become a potential dynasty that no one saw coming at the time. So if Malone sees special things in his current team, it\u2019s his responsibility to shout about it every now and then, both to the basketball public and especially internally. Youngsters like Jokic and Murray, Gary Harris and Malik Beasley, Torrey Craig and Mont Morris and even veterans like Paul Millsap, Mason Plumlee and Game 4 hero Will Barton, who knocked down huge shots to help seal the deal, need to hear the positive reinforcement from their coach. And that\u2019s not even taking into account what absorbing these moments means for Michael Porter Jr., who is spending his rookie season recovering from back surgery, and is certainly going to be a part of that bright future Malone is so passionate about. If anything, this Nuggets team is ahead of schedule, two wins shy of a trip to the Western Conference finals with three games to play. Two of those are coming on their home floor, where Denver compiled the best record (34-7) in the league during the regular season. Maybe Malone is right to speak the Nuggets\u2019 success into existence rather than wishing and hoping for it to come to fruition without a word otherwise. But he won\u2019t have to go all car salesmen on the final day of month much longer. A couple more performances like the one the Nuggets put on Sunday and this whole thing, the refurbished franchise with all the boxes checked on the roster \u2014 now and for the foreseeable future \u2014 sells itself. * * * Sekou Smith is a veteran NBA reporter and NBA TV analyst. And so we just created a conversation as long as the objection, and that\u2019s what we discussed in great detail.\u201d Not likely. Stevens probably talked with Irving about some of the things the coach had said to reporters Saturday and Sunday: the need for the Celtics not to settle and not to succumb to whatever chaos Milwaukee\u2019s defense was instigating by getting chaotic themselves. For two days, answering various questions, Stevens has steered the discussion to Boston moving the ball as the surest way to high-quality shots. \u201cOne of the things that we have to do as a team is just make those right reads off the first drive,\u201d Stevens said, \u201cand then go from there. We do have to do a better job of getting the ball to the second side of the floor, to the third side of the floor, and hopefully that includes as many paint attacks as possible.\u201d The Bucks\u2019 unveiled a switching style of defense in Game 2, something they hadn\u2019t done much all season. Too often, Irving\u2019s eyes lit up seeing center Brook Lopez or forward Nikola Mirotic isolated in front of him, and the tunnel vision that triggered didn\u2019t even generate the best shots for Irving. Too seldom, the Celtics failed to get Milwaukee\u2019s defense shifting left-to-right, and back again. \u201cWe have to make sure we\u2019re patient in getting the best looks,\u201d Stevens said Sunday. \u201cThat patience doesn\u2019t mean you ease into it. You have to work really hard and be patient against these guys. Because they do a great job of covering up the paint.\u201d Celtics players made only 9-of-19 attempts inside 5 feet, a feeble rate by NBA standards. Lopez has been one of the league\u2019s best defending the rim this season, and Giannis Antetokounmpo has the mobility to guard his man away from the hoop and still help inside. Admirably, Irving has been willing to shoulder responsibility, flatly stating after the Game 2 loss: \u201cThis is what I signed up for. This is what Boston traded for me for.\u201d After each game, he has given detailed breakdowns of what\u2019s gone right, what\u2019s gone wrong and, most important, what he planned to do in the next game. It\u2019s just that his most recent results haven\u2019t matched his pledges. The Celtics have more talent than the Bucks, and more players capable of carrying them offensively through a game or just a pivotal stretch. But those scorers, from Jayson Tatum and Jaylen Brown to Al Horford and Gordon Hayward, are dependent on Irving finding them and delivering the ball in a rhythm. Admittedly, Boston has to cinch things up defensively. Averaging 110 points ought to be enough, if you\u2019re not getting blistered at a 39-percent rate from 3-point range while sending your opponents to the free throw line an average of 30 times nightly. Irving blamed all those stoppages for stealing transition opportunities and generally messing with the Celtics\u2019 offense. Even so, he must do what Stevens calls \u201ccontrolling the controllables\u201d and be his best self. He has gotten the better of his individual matchup with Milwaukee point guard Eric Bledsoe, but that\u2019s not enough. And yes, this is what he signed up for. The great unknowns of this offseason, as in Irving\u2019s future whereabouts, could swing wildly on what happens in the coming days. And what he makes happen. \u201cI\u2019ve got to be me. That\u2019s the easy part,\u201d Irving said Sunday. \u201cThe same mindset that I\u2019ve always had. Being aggressive. Being patient. Being able to be aware of the time of the game and where I need to make my impact. Being able to be in the right defensive positions and being able to communicate with my teammates as well. \u201cThat\u2019s the easy part though. That\u2019s the fun part, if you go out there and just allow the flow of the game to dictate your instincts.\u201d * * * Steve Aschburner has written about the NBA since 1980. The Raptors prevailed in Game 4, a 101-96 victory that evened the series at 2-2, because Kawhi Leonard continues to play absurd basketball. Sunday\u2019s damage: 39 points, 14 rebounds and five assists, along with the biggest shot of the series so far, a pull-up 3-pointer over Joel Embiid at the shot-clock buzzer that put the Raptors up 94-90 with 1:01 to go. Through four games, Leonard is averaging 38 points on a ridiculous effective field goal percentage of 69 percent. That effective field goal percentage, with more than half (51\/89) of his shots having come from outside the paint, is better than anybody in the league shot on at least 300 field goal attempts this season \u2013 with the top seven guys being bigs who took almost all of their shots in the paint. \u201cThe stuff that he can do to create his own shot,\u201d said Sixers coach Brett Brown, \u201cis Kobe-like for me. He\u2019s just so gifted in relation to doing that.\u201d Nobody else on the Raptors has an effective field goal percentage that even reaches the league average (52.4 percent) in the series. Pascal Siakam (50.0 percent) is the closest , but was clearly suffering from his bruised right calf on Sunday, shooting 2-for-10, including 0-for-7 outside the restricted area. So, with or without a healthy Siakam, Raptors\u2019 coach Nick Nurse has been searching for answers \u2013 \u201cYou\u2019re looking for some type of spark,\u201d he said pre-game \u2013 someone or something he could count on, knowing that Leonard could not beat the Sixers by himself. Patrick McCaw was introduced to the non-garbage-time portion of the series on Sunday, but the big change for Toronto in Game 4 was Toronto playing \u2026 big. Since the start of this season, Serge Ibaka has almost exclusively played center. After starting alongside Jonas Valanciunas each of the last two seasons, Ibaka played just 13 total minutes with Valanciunas before the trade deadline. After Valanciunas was dealt to Memphis for Marc Gasol, Ibaka and Gasol played just 31 regular-season minutes together . Through eight playoff games, Ibaka and Gasol were on the floor together for just three total minutes . And through the first three games of this series, they had each shot 6-for-20 , with Ibaka registering a minus-17 in his 49 minutes. But with 5:27 to go in the first quarter on Sunday, Ibaka checked in for Siakam instead of Gasol. And it wasn\u2019t just about Siakam\u2019s injury, because the starting power forward played almost 29 minutes on Sunday, including a couple of minutes in a do-or-die fourth quarter with both Ibaka and Gasol on the floor and Leonard playing the two. When Siakam has sat, the Raptors have typically played Leonard or Norman Powell at the 4. On Sunday, Nurse chose to go big, quite an adjustment to make with the Raptors facing a 3-1 series deficit if it didn\u2019t work. \u201cTonight was one of those nights,\u201d Ibaka said, \u201cwhere we say, \u2018You know what? Even though we didn\u2019t have an opportunity to play together a long time, we\u2019re going to just try it out there.\u2019 We\u2019ve been playing basketball for so long. We\u2019re just going to try to figure it out and play hard.\u201d The two bigs played more than 23 minutes together on Sunday, with almost half of that coming in the fourth quarter, when the Raptors\u2019 season was seemingly on the line. The game was tied at 75 entering the fourth and Toronto was down four after the first few Philly possessions. But Nurse stuck with the two bigs and actually played them with Siakam and Leonard for a couple of minutes in that fourth quarter. The risk paid off. Ibaka and Gasol combined to hit three big shots, they spaced the floor well enough to let Leonard do his thing, and the Raptors held Philly to just 5-for-21 shooting in the final period to regain homecourt advantage in the series. \u201cThe biggest thing I felt tonight was the rebounding,\u201d Nurse said, of the bigs playing together. \u201cIt just felt like we were getting pushed around a lot by the glass the last two games. That would happen with our small lineup, they were just throwing it up there and revving their engines and flying to the rim. Tonight we just had more size, that way, and it kind of looked like the rebounds were affected by that.\u201d The Raptors\u2019 defensive rebounding wasn\u2019t any better in the Ibaka-Gasol minutes (when they grabbed 16 defensive rebounds and the Sixers\u2019 grabbed five offensive boards) that it was otherwise (19 and six). But it was better than having Siakam on the bench and a smaller power forward on the floor through the first four games (18 18 defensive rebounds , 10 Philadelphia  offensive boards ). With the rebounding improvement, the Sixers scored just 40 points on 44 possessions with Ibaka and Gasol on the floor together. They scored 56 points on 50 possessions otherwise. On the other end, Gasol (7-for-13) and Ibaka (6-for-12) each made as many shots as they had made through the first three games. It was just enough support for Leonard for the Raptors to get the biggest win of their season. \u201cHe\u2019s comfortable being a 5,\u201d Gasol said of Ibaka. \u201cI\u2019m able to play both positions a little bit. So it\u2019s simple. Defensively we mix it up, and the actions that they try to run we just need to figure out, and talk, and communicate and be on the same page, not just the two of us, but the whole team.\u201d Philly\u2019s output was affected by another ailment befalling Joel Embiid, who felt ill all day and shot just 2-for-7. The Sixers still outscored the Raptors by 17 points in Embiid\u2019s 35 minutes, but they were unable to survive the 13 minutes Embiid was on the bench: the Raptors won those handily, 33-11. \u201cWe\u2019re all going to look at what he shot from the floor, his free throws, whatever,\u201d Brown said of his center. \u201cCut to the chase, go to the bottom line, and look at his plus-minus. Despite him being ill and despite seven shots, or what he shot at the free throw line, and really his free throws were quite good up until the fourth period, he ends up a plus-17. It\u2019s just another reminder of how important he is to our team.\u201d Maybe that\u2019s the big story from this game. But Embiid\u2019s plus-17 breaks down to a plus-1 in the 20 minutes he played with both Ibaka and Gasol on the floor , and a plus-16 in fewer than 15 minutes against just one of two. The NBA playoffs are different than the 82-game grind of the regular season. Sometimes, they require something that you\u2019ve never done before. In Game 4 on Sunday, the Raptors went big for the first time this season and, as Nurse put it, \u201cdid just enough\u201d to get a crucial victory. * * * John Schuhmann is a senior stats analyst for NBA.com. Kevin Durant \u201cis the best player in the world, the most skilled player in the world\u201d according to Kerr, who may be biased, but he didn\u2019t sound like it. Durant is evidently on a mission to (a) win his third and perhaps final championship with the Warriors, and (1-a) become universally recognized as the singularly greatest force in the league, a distinction that means so much to him. To paraphrase Durant, y\u2019all know who he is by now. Durant is sitting at the mythical 50-40-90 threshold in the playoffs, the benchmark for shooting accuracy and efficiency from the floor, 3-point range and free-throw line. He\u2019s averaging 35 points in the postseason, 39 in the last seven games. He has two near-masterpieces, the 50-point closeout of the Clippers in the first round and 46 on the Rockets in Game 3 of this series . He\u2019s making contested jumpers from all over the floor and from all angles. There\u2019s really no defense for him. But when this series is over, James Harden hopes to change the conversation. Well, Harden might be the only player qualified to do so, even with a left eye that still looks like the Japanese flag. We just have to try to make it hard as possible for him.\u201d The nightmare game for the Warriors is Harden hitting enough early baskets and forcing them to double, then finding teammates for open looks that they make, such as Eric Gordon. In that scenario, points would come in an avalanche and place stress on the defense and possibly get key players into foul trouble, most notably Draymond Green and a suddenly-foul-prone Steph Curry. There\u2019s also an intriguing subplot in the works: The Harden-Durant can-you-top-this drama. There\u2019s a genuine and hefty amount of respect between the two, who are friends away from the floor as well. Both left OKC and have since generated millions in endorsement money and find themselves near or at the top of the superstar pecking order. Durant has what Harden doesn\u2019t, a championship. But perhaps Harden has what Durant craves, a team to call his own. That would be the only reason Durant leaves the Warriors in free agency this summer, because it\u2019s difficult to imagine him signing with a team that offers a better chance to win championships or make more in salary than the one he\u2019s already on. Durant added: \u201cHe can do everything. If you\u2019re not focused, he can drive past you, hit you with the shoulder because he\u2019s strong, and finish with either hand. He can shoot floaters now. Obviously the step-back 3-pointer is one of his staples, but I never believed he was just a free throw guy. He can score in a variety of ways.\u201d Harden must prove that in this series. Last season in the Western Conference finals, he turned to vapor as that series stretched seven games. He made just 24 percent from deep and, after Paul suffered a hamstring pull in Game Five, couldn\u2019t handle the load. In the elimination game, he missed 11-of-13 from deep. Durant, meanwhile, was the star and weeks later would clinch another title and Finals MVP award, outplaying LeBron James in the process. So Kerr\u2019s contention about Durant has much weight and credibility. Through three games of this second-round series, there\u2019s been no reason to question the coach\u2019s claim. "},{"id":33938,"link":"https:\/\/www.nba.com\/news\/toronto-raptors-looking-more-offense-pivotal-game-4","date":"2019-05-05","title":"Raptors looking for more offense in pivotal Game 4","content":"It\u2019s gut-check time for the Toronto Raptors. Their season isn\u2019t over if they lose Game 4 of their Eastern Conference semifinals series with the Philadelphia 76ers on Sunday ( 3:30 p.m. ET, ABC ), but this is as pivotal as a Game 4 can get. A win draws the Raptors even at 2-2, with two of the last three games in Toronto. A loss puts them in a 3-1 hole that few teams have climbed out of. Most of the Toronto rotation has played in bigger games. Kawhi Leonard, Danny Green and Serge Ibaka have all played in The Finals. Marc Gasol, Kyle Lowry and Norman Powell, meanwhile, have played in the conference finals. But this is a new level of stress for a new group and a new head coach. In a series between two teams that went all-in for this season, the Raptors are the first to come face-to-face with their own mortality. To win on Sunday, the Raptors will need to slow down the Sixers\u2019 best player while providing more help for their own. And they may have to do it without Pascal Siakam, who is listed as doubtful for Game 4 with a right calf contusion. In this series, Leonard and Siakam have combined to average 61 points per game . All other Raptors have combined to averaged just 36.3. After Game 1, it was easy to point out the flaws of the Sixers\u2019 rotation. Three of their five starters couldn\u2019t guard Leonard, and their bench was painfully shallow. But two games later, the Sixers have never looked better (or more cohesive) and it\u2019s the Raptors that seem terminally flawed, especially on offense, where they\u2019ve scored less than 97 points per 100 possessions over Games 2 and 3. For the series, Toronto is down to 101.4 points scored per 100 possessions, the worst mark in the conference semis and down from their rate of 111.5 per 100 in the first round . The Raptors have a higher free throw rate than they did in the first round . They\u2019ve turned the ball less often than they did against Orlando and their offensive rebounding percentage has only dropped a bit. Their offensive struggles have been mostly about the biggest drop in effective field goal percentage from the first round (55.0 percent) to the conference semis (48.4 percent). Two key areas on the floor have hurt the Raptors: The Raptors\u2019 shooting issues begin early in the shot clock. In the regular season, Toronto averaged a league-best 1.19 points per possession in transition , according to Synergy tracking. That number is down to just 1.01 points per possession in the playoffs , with only Orlando and Oklahoma City having scored less efficiently in transition. The Raptors\u2019 transition issues haven\u2019t begun in the conference semis. They scored 86 points on 85 transition possessions (1.01) in the first round and have scored 62 on 62 (1.00 per) against the Sixers. But they have shot worse early in the clock than they did against Orlando. According to Second Spectrum tracking, the Raptors\u2019 have an effective field goal percentage of just 43.6 percent in the first six seconds of the shot clock in the conference semis, down from 54.1 percent in the first round and 63.1 percent (the league\u2019s second best mark) in the regular season. Effective field goal percentage is typically highest in the first six seconds of the clock, but for the Raptors in this series, it\u2019s been lower than it\u2019s been in the middle 12 seconds (49.7 percent) or in the last six seconds of the clock (48.9 percent). Credit the Sixers and their size. Most of those 2-point misses in the first six seconds of the clock have been contested layups. The Raptors have shot just 55.4 percent at the rim when there\u2019s been a Sixer there to defend it . The Raptors clearly need more from Leonard\u2019s supporting cast, especially if Siakam can\u2019t play on Sunday. And the obvious culprits in regard to lack of offensive production are Kyle Lowry and Marc Gasol. Through Friday\u2019s games, there were 39 players with at least 100 touches in the conference finals, and while Leonard led the 39 with 0.604 points per touch , Gasol (0.132) and Lowry (0.130) ranked 35th and 36th. Fred VanVleet (0.040) ranked last. The all need to shoot better. Through three games, Lowry has shot 36 percent, Gasol has shot 30 percent, and VanVleet is 1-for-11. They also might need to get more touches. According to Second Spectrum, Lowry has averaged 33 front-court touches per 36 minutes in the conference semis, down from 42 per 36 in the first round. Gasol has averaged 41, down from 46. And VanVleet has averaged 23 per 36, down from 37 per 36 in the first round. Leonard has had the ball a lot more than usual. He has averaged 5.0 minutes of possession against Philly, up from 3.9 in the first round and 4.0 in the regular season . The 5.0 still only ranks 13th in this round of the playoffs (though that\u2019s partly because Denver and Portland played four overtimes on Friday). * * * John Schuhmann is a senior stats analyst for NBA.com. Back and forth they went, the Portland Trail Blazers and Denver Nuggets, in these Western Conference semifinals from a sun-soaked at the start Moda Center Friday evening until nearly midnight and into the early morning hours Saturday. Four grueling overtimes, filled with enough gasp-inducing moments, enough emotionally jarring plays for both teams to win or lose this game a dozen times over. And that\u2019s exactly what it felt like when the longest playoff game of the shot-clock era was over. Let the record show that Seth Curry\u2019s free throws with 2.8 seconds to play sealed the deal for the Trail Blazers in the final seconds of a wild 140-137 win, the NBA\u2019s first four-overtime playoff game since 1953. But it was the fresh legs and smooth flick of the left wrist of Rodney Hood that provided the crease of separation the Trail Blazers needed to finally end this marathon game, the 3,986th playoff game in NBA history, by the way, a game Nuggets coach Michael Malone dubbed an \u2018instant classic.\u201d Could there have been a more unlikely hero for the Trail Blazers, who now own a 2-1 lead in this series with Game 4 looming Sunday afternoon, than Hood? Or a bigger scapegoat than Denver superstar big man Nikola Jokic, who with an opportunity to tie the game with 5.6 seconds left, missed the first of two free throws? \u201cIn that moment, you don\u2019t think you are tired or how much you have played,\u201d Jokic said after logging a mind-boggling 65 minutes. \u201cYou just want to give everything you\u2019ve got in that moment. You don\u2019t think about the exhaustion or how tired you are.\u201d When you own the trust of your coaches and teammates, when you believe and trust in each other the way the Blazers clearly do, you rise to the magnitude of the moment the way Hood did. So maybe it does make sense, that he came through the way he did. In fact, it might have been the only thing that made any sense on the wildest night yet of this NBA postseason. \u201cI just felt pretty good,\u201d Hood said. \u201cI was telling Gary Trent that if I got the chance, I was going to make it. They bring me in to be a fresh hand, and I can make a difference. So I was excited to go back in the fourth overtime.\u201d Hood\u2019s pull-up 14-footer with 1:07 to play in that fourth overtime tied the game at 133-133. A 12-footer with 44.9 to play put the Blazers up 135-134 after Will Barton, the Nuggets\u2019 fresh-legged alternative, made one of two free throws to put the Nuggets ahead seconds earlier. Hood\u2019s dagger with 18.6 seconds left and the Blazers down 136-135 after a Paul Millsap hook put the Nuggets ahead again. Hood stroked his spot-up 26-footer with the confidence of a player who was built for these magical moments, guys like Blazers\u2019 stars Damian Lillard and CJ McCollum, who had no problem deferring to Hood or anyone else given the fatigue the starters on both sides were experiencing four overtimes in. \u201cIt\u2019s up there, it\u2019s one of the biggest moments and obviously it\u2019s the playoffs,\u201d Hood said in regards to where his big shot ranks on his personal all-time list. \u201cI\u2019ve hit game-winners and big shots before, but this one means a lot, especially going back into the game after being out for a while.\u201d For a player like Hood, whose personal redemption tour fits perfectly with the redemptive ride these Blazers are on in this postseason, the timing couldn\u2019t have been better. The Blazers were swept out of the playoffs in the first round last season and the season before, so their grind to try to reach the conference finals this season is real. Hood, meanwhile, was in Cleveland this time a year ago, making headlines for all the wrong reasons. He infamously refused to check into Game 4 of the Cavaliers\u2019 series against Toronto, unhappy with his fit and role after being traded from Utah in February to a LeBron James-led team that would make The Finals for a fourth straight season. Having a chance to come through in the clutch Friday night was a completely different feeling for Hood, who joined this crew after a February trade that sent Nik Stauskas , Wade Baldwin and second-round picks in 2021 and 2023 to Cleveland in the exchange. \u201cIt means a lot,\u201d Hood said. \u201cI stayed patient. I didn\u2019t get down on myself even when a lot of people did \u2026 trusting God, I just didn\u2019t give up, didn\u2019t give up on myself. I just kept playing, kept working. I had no idea I\u2019d end up in Portland in this type of situation. But to be here and to have my family in the crowd. I saw all the sad faces the last go-around, so to be here is very emotional for me, to be quite frank. Got a lot more basketball to get through right now. A lot more basketball to go.\u201d That\u2019s hard to imagine after living through the second quadruple overtime game in NBA history, the first since 1953. Jokic played a game-high 65 minutes; only three players in NBA history have played more minutes in a postseason game \u2014 Red Rocha (67), Paul Seymour (67) and Bob Cousy (66) in that 1953 four-overtime game. But Jamal Murray also played 55, Millsap 49 and Gary Harris nearly 50 before fouling out after a night of stellar defense on Lillard. McCollum led the Blazers in minutes (franchise-record 60) and points (41), while Lillard logged 58 minutes, Enes Kanter 57 (with that busted shoulder), Al-Farouq Aminu 47 and Maurice Harkless 45. The wear and tear, physically and emotionally, was on full display after the game. That goes for the players and the coaches alike, not to mention the roller-coaster ride the sellout crowd went through with each and every exhilarating and deflating moment inside the building. \u201cI have no idea what happened in the first half, the second half, or the first three overtimes,\u201d Blazers coach Terry Stotts said. \u201cRodney Hood came in and played great. It was a hell of a game. I\u2019ve never been involved in a game like that, regular season or playoffs, but it was an amazing effort by both teams.\u201d Stotts\u2019 decision to go to Hood in that fourth overtime wasn\u2019t necessarily coaching genius either. It was also a product of the collective fatigue of the starters playing wicked minutes. \u201cMo was cramping up a little bit,\u201d Stotts said, \u201cso that was the reason for the substitution. But we did go to Hood a couple times. Obviously, the 3-point shot was a scramble situation. CJ had a great play, but we did go to Hood because we liked the matchup a couple of times and he had fresh legs.\u201d And he had the trust of his teammates, the guys who had to facilitate in the heat of the moment to provide Hood\u2019s chance to play the hero. Lillard is as dialed in a leader as there is in this league. He knew exactly what it meant when Hood came in and out during those overtimes periods and delivered the way he did. \u201cIt was huge,\u201d he said. \u201cLooking back at the first round, I don\u2019t think he had the kind of series that he wanted to have, but in the series we had different guys come in and come up big for the team. I think in this series, every game he\u2019s come in and given us a huge lift. I thought tonight was fitting that he was able to come through big for us and it was at a big time in the game. \u201cAt one point, we were down two and coach called a play for him and he scored it and the next time, I was like \u2018same play\u2019 and he looked at me like \u2018what are we doing?\u2019 And I was like, \u2018same thing, let\u2019s keep it rolling.\u2019 And he hit another one. I think that we he did for our team tonight was huge. Same thing in Game 2, but I think us trusting him and continuing to play through him in big moments, it made him feel that much better about that 3-pointer he shot. He rose up with confidence and hit a big shot, so it\u2019s been great to see him coming for the team, especially tonight.\u201d Tonight bled into this morning for the weary on both sides, and before you know it Sunday afternoon and Game 4 will be staring them all in the face. Like Stotts, Malone said he\u2019s not sure where he\u2019ll find the time to make sure his crew recovers properly and in time for what is essentially the most important game of the season for both teams. \u201cThey have the same turnaround,\u201d he said. \u201cI mean, Kanter played 56, McCollum played 60 \u2026 we\u2019re talking about a playoff game and guys are playing 60 minutes. That\u2019s crazy. So yeah, you give them rest. You let them sleep in, you get them treatment, rehab, recovery \u2026 you try to learn from the loss and get ready to battle. Both teams are exhausted, so its the same for them as it is for us. We will not use that as an excuse. In a late-November game between North Carolina and Texas played in Las Vegas, the Tar Heels senior forward Luke Maye, on the dead run just outside the free-throw line, split two Longhorn defenders and grabbed a perfect pass from freshman point guard Coby White. He seemed bound for an easy layup. But before Maye could complete the play, Jaxson Hayes , Texas\u2019 6-foot-11 freshman center with a 7-foot-3 wingspan, made up about six feet of separation with one stride, left the floor outside the restricted area arc and pinned Mayes\u2019 shot against the backboard with two hands. \u201cJust the ferociousness and violence with which he blocked the shot,\u201d says Texas coach Shaka Smart, his voice trailing off as his mind pulled up the image of Maye wondering how the hell a sure basket had been snuffed out. \u201cIt was just one block. Just one play. But you were like, \u2018Yeah, he\u2019s gonna play in the NBA for a long time. It\u2019s just a matter of when.\u2019 \u201d Michigan State associate head coach Dwayne Stephens, who had been scouting the game because Texas was the Spartans\u2019 opponent the next day, seemed much more certain about Hayes\u2019 timetable when he ran into his good friend, former Texas associate head coach Darrin Horn. \u201cThe first words out of his mouth,\u201d says Horn, now the head coach at Northern Kentucky, \u201cwere \u2018Yeah, ya\u2019ll won\u2019t be able to keep him.\u2019 There were like 35 NBA scouts there. No question that was an ah-ha moment for everybody.\u201d Sure enough, there was more to come from Hayes. Texas under Smart has gotten the reputation of producing one-and-done big men \u2014 Jarrett Allen was the 22nd pick in the 2017 draft and Mo Bama was the No. 6 a year ago \u2014 and Hayes is going to join them after a season in which he averaged 10 points and five rebounds, was voted Big 12 Freshman of the Year, second-team All-Big 12 and chosen to the league\u2019s All-Freshman, All-Newcomer and All-Defensive teams. Hayes announced early last month after helping Texas win the NIT he was entering the NBA Draft, where, if the various mock drafts are any indication, he\u2019ll be a lottery pick. \u201cHe just kept getting better and better,\u201d Smart says. \u201cThere\u2019s no reason to think he won\u2019t continue to do that.\u201d NBA scouts and general managers make their bones by judging potential. For every Kobe Bryant, LeBron James, or, in the 2019 Draft, Zion Williamson \u2014 can\u2019t miss prospects \u2014 there are countless others whose NBA futures have to be projected. Though Hayes earned all those accolades, he\u2019s not in Williamson\u2019s class \u2014 even though Hayes blocked one of Williamson\u2019s shots in an AAU game \u2014 and didn\u2019t leave behind a ton of gaudy statistics. \u201cClearly the NBA guys see the potential,\u201d Smart says. \u201cThis guy didn\u2019t have a double-double [in points and rebounds] all year. You can do the research, but I challenge you to find a 6-11 kid who\u2019s going to be a top-10 pick that never had a double-double. I\u2019m not saying that negatively. Some guys are picked for what they can do in a shorter term. The bigger they are, the longer they are, it\u2019s more about potential.\u201d Hayes did put up some impressive numbers. He finished third in the Big 12 in blocked shots. He shot 82.3 from the free-throw line (51-for-62) in league games. And he obliterated the Texas single-season record for field-goal percentage (72.8). Dexter Pittman shot 65.4 in 2009-10. Allen\u2019s success ratio from the floor was 72.9. Of course, Allen dunked a lot. Smart and Horn, who coached the Texas big men, kept things relatively simple for Hayes, in part because they thought they would have him longer and didn\u2019t want to overload him as a rookie. Hayes took only one jump shot all season. But the Texas coaches also knew they had a weapon in Hayes\u2019 athleticism. \u201cOf the three big kids we had the last three years, Jaxson is the best mover of the bunch,\u201d Horn says. \u201cJarrett\u2019s a terrific sprinter, but Jaxson is right there. Mo\u2019s skilled, he can shoot, and he\u2019s a really good passer. But in terms of being a basketball mover \u2014 quickness, sprinting, moving in space \u2026 being able to catch the ball on the roll and contort his body because a help defender has slid in front of him, stuff like that, he\u2019s the best mover of the bunch. The most athletically gifted.\u201d Texas teaches all its bigs to play on balance, to compete and to get in position to explode to the rim. Hayes didn\u2019t need much coaching on the latter point. \u201cWe had no idea how good he would be offensively from the standpoint of when he was rolling to the rim or on the move in transition,\u201d Horn says. \u201cHe could take the ball, and in one fluid motion flush it. Where most guys, even if they have good hands and can catch it, they have to gather themselves. Jaxson can catch that thing and get off the floor so quickly in stride before anyone knows what happened and be dunking on you.\u201d Besides the obvious, it\u2019s easy to see why NBA scouts have been intrigued by Hayes\u2019 potential. His father Jonathan played 12 seasons in the NFL as a 6-foot-5, 250-pound tight end. His mother Kristi played college basketball at Drake and in her senior season of high school in Iowa averaged 52 points. Hayes didn\u2019t follow in his mother\u2019s footsteps, at least not right away. He played football at famed Archbishop Moeller High School in Cincinnati until he grew so tall that, as a wide receiver, he was too big a target for opposing defensive backs. Switching to basketball seemed a more natural fit, but he wasn\u2019t instantly successful. Hayes didn\u2019t start as a junior and averaged about six minutes and two points a game. But his size and athleticism nevertheless attracted attention. The first school to offer a scholarship was Middle Tennessee State. Before he became the 2018-19 Southeastern Conference coach of the year at Ole Miss, Kermit Davis spent 16 years at Middle Tennessee, where he put together some great teams by having a keen eye for hidden talent. Davis remembers the first time he saw Hayes on film after getting tipped off by a friend in Ohio. Hayes had just completed his junior season. \u201cI watched two minutes of video, picked up the phone and called his high school coach and offered a scholarship,\u201d Davis says. Hayes and his father visited Middle Tennessee, and Davis and assistant coach Ronnie Hamilton had high hopes of nabbing Hayes before anyone else could. Then came summer AAU ball. \u201cWe were there for his first AAU game that spring when he played against Zion Williamson,\u201d says Hamilton, who\u2019s now on Davis\u2019 staff at Ole Miss. \u201cHe didn\u2019t score a lot but battled against Zion, ran the floor like a deer, rebounded the ball. Coach Davis and I said he was going to soar past the Conference USA level.\u201d Soar past it Hayes did. By the end of the summer, Hayes, who had barely played as a junior, had more than 40 scholarship offers, many from power conference schools. One of them was Texas. \u201cIt took me like 12 seconds,\u201d Horn says. \u201cI watched him run the floor, and then back down the floor on the other end, where he grabbed a ball that came off the rim kind of sideways \u2014 just launched up and grabbed one that nobody else on the floor could even come close to. I called Shaka and said \u2018Coach, just how this dude moves alone, yes, he\u2019s good enough.\u2019 \u201d Hayes and his family liked Smart and his coaches and were impressed by Texas\u2019 development of big men. Little did anyone realize how fast Hayes would develop and be gone for the NBA. But the signs were all around, even early in the season. \u201cAfter watching him a ton the spring and summer before his senior year of high school, I thought he would definitely be an NBA guy, but that it might take two or three years,\u201d Hamilton says. \u201cSo, I was a little surprised how quickly he adapted to the college game. I remember when I was scouting Georgia \u2014 Texas had played them \u2014 and watching film. I said to myself, \u2018Jaxson\u2019s a lottery guy now.\u2019\u201d So how far can Hayes progress in the NBA? Smart thinks a long way. \u201cIt\u2019s a very safe bet that he\u2019s going to have a ridiculous body [Hayes played at about 220 pounds this season] in the next three to five years,\u201d Smart says. \u201cHis dad is a mountain of a man. Obviously, the NBA people see that. \u201cAs far as his game, he\u2019s going to be able to make jumpers. He\u2019s going to put the ball on the floor. The way the NBA\u2019s going, he\u2019ll probably eventually shoot 3s. The kid\u2019s just a puppy. Who knows what he\u2019s capable of doing?\u201d * * * Chris Dortch is the editor of the Blue Ribbon College Basketball Yearbook . But the first three games of the Eastern Conference semifinals series between the Toronto Raptors and the Philadelphia 76ers have felt like an evolution. Philadelphia\u2019s wire-to-wire, 116-95 victory in Game 3 was a new team finding itself on both ends of the floor and putting together its best performance of the year. Neither of these teams entered this series with a clear sense of who they were. The Raptors\u2019 starting lineup had played together in just 19 games prior to Game 1, while the Sixers\u2019 starting lineup had played together in just 14. The Raptors had Kawhi Leonard, and that was enough in Game 1. But since then, Toronto\u2019s attack hasn\u2019t evolved beyond a whole lot of Leonard and some Pascal Siakam on the side. Leonard has averaged 37.7 points on an effective field goal percentage of 67 percent in the series. Siakam, for the most part, has come along for the ride, averaging 23.3 on an effective field goal percentage of 55 percent. After that, things have been dicey. The other Raptors have been unable \u2026 and somewhat unwilling \u2026 to score. On Thursday, after Leonard scored 14 points in a remarkable run in the third quarter, helping the Raptors shave an 18-point deficit down to seven, the Sixers adjusted and sent two defenders at him. \u201cYou had to do something,\u201d said Sixers coach Brett Brown. \u201cAnd we chose to go after him and get the ball out of his hands.\u201d That should have opened things up for Leonard\u2019s teammates, and when he gave up the ball, the Philly defense was in rotation. But Toronto was unable to take advantage. The ball moved, but the Raptors didn\u2019t make (or even take) enough open shots. \u201cIt felt like there was a lot of guys passing up shots,\u201d said Raptors coach Nick Nurse. \u201cThere were a couple of possessions where I was like \u2018There it goes up\u2019 and four more passes would go by. It seemed like every guy that had it was open and he\u2019d move it to the next guy.\u201d \u201cI just think we\u2019re making the game harder than it has to be on ourselves,\u201d Danny Green said. \u201cSometimes, just make the easy play and go from there. Take the first open look or the first easy shot. A lot of times, we\u2019re over-swinging it or over-thinking it or taking too long to swing it.\u201d The Raptors simply aren\u2019t making the Sixers pay for the attention they\u2019ve put on Leonard, whether that be the double-teams they threw at him late in Game 3 or just showing him extra bodies on the strong side of the floor. \u201cWe\u2019ve got to help him,\u201d said Kyle Lowry, who has averaged 12 points and shot 2-for-14 from 3-point range in the series. \u201cWe\u2019ve got to help him. Myself especially, I\u2019ve got to help him score more. I\u2019ve got to help him on the floor. We\u2019ve all got to help him. He\u2019s playing unbelievable right now. We\u2019re not giving him any help. Me, I\u2019m not giving him any help. We\u2019ve got to help him.\u201d That was six got to help hims, by the way. Credit the Sixers\u2019 defense. Their rotations were on point, running some Raptors off of catch-and-shoot opportunities. And when Toronto attacked closeouts off the dribble and got into the paint, Joel Embiid was there to block their shots or scare them from even taking them. Embiid matched Leonard\u2019s 33 points in Game 3, shooting 3-for-4 from 3-point range and 12-for-13 from the free throw line. And he added 10 rebounds, three assists and five blocks, including two rejections of Siakam on a 21-2, fourth-quarter run that turned the seven-point lead into a 26-point cushion and brought out the third string for both teams. \u201c[Joel] is our crown jewel defensively,\u201d Brown said, \u201cand I suppose offensively too, but certainly defensively. And his rim protection and shot-blocking ability tonight stood as much as anything in an incredible performance.\u201d The defense was there in Game 2, when the Sixers won a game in which both teams scored less than a point per possession. The rapid evolution of this series really surfaced on the other end of the floor, where Philly scored 110 points on 85 possessions (1.29 per) before extended garbage time on Thursday. Embiid was the fulcrum of the offense as much as he was the defense. The 3-for-4 from 3-point range was an anomaly, but he also did a lot of work inside, both in the post and as a roll man. And with Philly playing more pick-and-roll than they\u2019ve done in the past, there were opportunities for Jimmy Butler and Tobias Harris off the dribble. For one of the first times since the Sixers\u2019 talented starting lineup came together, all five guys were able to get theirs on offense without anyone taking a real back seat. And while the Raptors are seemingly making things too complicated, the Sixers have seemingly just taken care of the basics, as Butler would put it when he was asked about the team\u2019s chemistry on pick-and-rolls. \u201cI think \u2018chemistry\u2019 is a bad word,\u201d Butler said. \u201cLike everybody wants to say that because we play together however many games or we didn\u2019t play together however many games. It\u2019s simple enough to know that when you have some good basketball players out there, the game happens, you make the right plays, you do what you\u2019re supposed to do with the basketball. That\u2019s all it is. The game is really simple. I think at times we as players decide to make it hard, but if you\u2019re open, shoot it. If you\u2019re not, pass.\u201d The Raptors would do well to follow Butler\u2019s advice in Game 4 on Sunday ( 3:30 ET, ABC ). If they don\u2019t, the evolution of this series could lead to the extinction of their season. * * * John Schuhmann is a senior stats analyst for NBA.com. From Eric Bledsoe and Kyrie Irving, ball handlers who have initiated much of the action, to a pair of valuable role players racing to get back from injuries, a half-dozen Bucks and Celtics are defining (or still hope to define) this series from the backcourt up: ERIC BLEDSOE has been the tip of Milwaukee\u2019s spear, the guy who typically sets the Bucks\u2019 pace and tone. He didn\u2019t play hard enough in the opener and the whole roster sagged. In Game 2, Bledsoe was aggressive, pressuring Irving relentlessly and channeling attack mode into 21 points and a plus-23 rating in the Bucks\u2019 blowout. Now Bledsoe is headed into TD Garden, site of his humbling in Games 1 and 2 last spring, with Celtics fans certain to remind him. KYRIE IRVING played a maestro\u2019s game Sunday, with 26 points and 11 assists while orchestrating and personally aiding Boston\u2019s 54-percent accuracy in its 112-90 cruise. He crashed and burned in Game 2, scoring just nine points on 4-of-18 shooting and relinquishing any quarterback obligations for his team. But the six-time All-Star was curiously at peace afterward, embracing the performance while touching in his postgame comments on precise adjustments he plans to make in Game 3 on Friday night and beyond. MALCOLM BROGDON is the guy conspicuous by his absence from the Bucks\u2019 efforts at both end. In his third NBA season, he emerged as a true sniper, making more than 50 percent of his field-goal attempts, 40 percent of his 3-pointers and 90 percent of his free throws. Brogdon\u2019s size allows him to defend multiple spots, and he developed a knack for asserting himself at pivotal points in close games, a minute or two where he\u2019d take over with his \u201cold-man game,\u201d as Antetokounmpo calls it. Until March 15, anyway. That\u2019s when the plantar fascia pain in Brogdon\u2019s right foot became a tear. He was listed out for Game 3, too, as of Thursday evening, but his workouts have been more active and encouraging, so Brogdon still might return as soon as Game 4 on Monday. MARCUS SMART is Brogdon\u2019s Boston counterpart, working back from a torn left oblique injury suffered in early April. He is a month into what had been projected as a 4-to-6 week recovery, and moved well in drills and 1-on-1 work Thursday against Celtics coaches. That\u2019s still well short of the 5-on-5, full-contact work Smart has to show he can handle. But as with Brogdon, it\u2019s trending in the right direction, even if both probably will need time to catch their rhythms. Boston can\u2019t get enough of the hustle and grit Smart routinely brings. He has been an unofficial coach from the side, urging on teammates in varied, essential, often floor-bound things he does. But the Celtics would be better with a healthy Smart showing rather than telling. TERRY ROZIER , who was such a force in the 2018 playoffs subbing for an injured Irving, isn\u2019t one to be counted out. It was Rozier who had Bledsoe on the run, with his memorable deke and step-back 3-pointer in Game 1, his 23 points in Game 2 and his 26 in the clincher. He\u2019s back on the bench, averaging 10.0 points and 8.5 rebounds through the first two games, while shooting just 6-of-18. Rozier was 21-of-55 from the arc in those seven games vs. Milwaukee last spring. Heading back into the Garden with the fans at his back, Rozier might catch fire again. GEORGE HILL was acquired from Cleveland in December as veteran insurance at the point and for salary cap flexibility this summer (only $1 million of his $18 million for 2019-20 is guaranteed). But he\u2019s an 11-year veteran with a broad wingspan for a 6-foot-3 man. And with 108 playoff games on his resume, Hill is a veteran of these East playoff battles, dating back to his time with Indiana butting heads against LeBron James\u2019 Super Friends. Hill and Ersan Ilyasova are the \u201cold heads\u201d of Milwaukee\u2019s otherwise youngish bench. Coach Mike Budenholzer had Ilyasova for a stretch in Atlanta, but he and Hill go way back. They were in San Antonio when the Spurs made Hill, out of Indiana University-Purdue University Indianapolis, their first-round pick at No. 26 in 2010. Hill was an apprentice behind All-Star Tony Parker for three years, starting only 55 of 231 games. But he developed enough to be targeted by the Pacers, who gave up the draft rights to Kawhi Leonard in the deal. \u201cThey kind of molded me to the player I am today,\u201d Hill said of Budenholzer and their then-boss Gregg Popovich. \u201cIt\u2019s not nothing special. I just try to be consistent. Make the right play. Be a good teammate. Be solid out there on both ends of the floor. That\u2019s been me my whole career, and it\u2019s going to continue to be.\u201d After joining Cleveland last season in a roster-rocking trade deadline move, Hill started 18 of 19 playoff games and made it to his first Finals. He opened this season putting up bigger numbers in the Cavs\u2019 dreary, LeBron-less outpost. But now he\u2019s back in heavy postseason competition, offering Budenholzer a nice shift of gears from the hard-charging Bledsoe. \u201cTwo different styles,\u201d Hill said. \u201cHe\u2019s more of a drive\/force cat, I\u2019m more of a poised, relaxed style. It\u2019s awesome to have two different options where you can speed it up or slow it down.\u201d The trust Budenholzer has in Hill is evident by when and how frequently the coach leans on him. \u201cIt\u2019s hard to articulate what George Hill means to the team,\u201d Budenholzer said. \u201cParticularly for me, just to have a point guard in Eric Bledsoe who starts the game and is a phenomenal defender, and then to have George come in, give Bled a break and really have no drop-off defensively. \u201cGeorge has played in a ton of huge games. A lot of playoff series. He gives me a ton of comfort. I think he gives his teammates and everybody a lot of comfort, for sure.\u201d * * * Steve Aschburner has written about the NBA since 1980. Or perhaps his man crush runs deep. In any event, Joe Lacob, the co-owner of the Warriors, did something unbefitting of a smart businessman whose personal net worth is into the billions. He ceded leverage. He violated Business 101 when he was quoted as saying this about the Warriors\u2019 jack-of-all-trades player: \u201cI want Draymond Green to be here forever. He\u2019s as Warrior as they come.\u201d There\u2019s no question about that last part; Green has spent his entire career with the Warriors and embodies the spirit, though not the superstardom, of a team currently surfing the wave of a four-championship dynasty. Green brings what Stephen Curry and Kevin Durant can\u2019t: An ability to guard multiple positions at a high level, and fire-breathing that puts a flame to everyone\u2019s backside and keeps their eyes on the prize. Asked how he felt about Lacob\u2019s comments, a smiling Green said \u201cit makes you feel good.\u201d He wants to be here. He\u2019s the definition of The Missing Piece, a player who brings all of the components together, doesn\u2019t shrink from big moments, willing to put everything on the line. That\u2019s unteachable stuff. You either have it or not. Yet, for everything that Green has meant to the Warriors then and even now, that doesn\u2019t mean he\u2019ll be at this level, in the gushing words of Lacob, \u201cforever.\u201d Maybe not even another year or two. Basically: Should the Warriors, who\u2019ll be forced to take action on Green\u2019s future soon enough, keep this often-turbulent marriage going and ignore the flashing caution signs all season that Green\u2019s impact is starting to wane? Sometimes we become slaves to the here and the now, because that\u2019s a fallacy in today\u2019s microwaved world, and the last impression Green left on the Warriors was indeed inspiring and hopeful. He did a number on the Rockets with his rebounding, his smart and tenacious defense and his passing, and that\u2019s a major reason the Warriors are up 2-0 and halfway to the West finals. This was Green in his element, causing problems for the other bench, bringing energy and grit and making sharp decisions on both ends. \u201cHe\u2019s really good,\u201d said Rockets coach Mike D\u2019Antoni. \u201cKind of a point guard at the center or forward position. He\u2019s a heck of a player. He moves really well.\u201d This was, without question, the best Green has looked all season, and maybe the last two seasons. His 3-point shot, once a weapon, is now dreadful enough that opposing teams are praying he shoots them \u2014 and thrilled when he makes one, because then he\u2019ll keep shooting. The Warriors\u2019 defense hasn\u2019t been as strong as it was in the first few seasons under coach Steve Kerr, and while that\u2019s not entirely the fault of Green, he\u2019s guilty by association if nothing else. There\u2019s also the pain-in-the-rear-end factor. While the Warriors obviously value Green\u2019s ability to hold teammates accountable, he went overboard earlier this season by his infamous confrontation with Durant and continues to rack up technical fouls at a high rate, meaning he hasn\u2019t learned how to channel his emotions after all these years. Plus, he continues to be high maintenance for Kerr, and you wonder if the coach will reach a point where he no longer wants to deal with it. Green is also 29 and brings an escalated injury history, most notably with his shoulder, but also toe and hip issues. \u201cAll this year I don\u2019t think I was in great shape at all,\u201d Green said. \u201cI\u2019m really just locking in and focusing on my body; getting it healthy and ready for this time of year has been important. It feels good to step out there and not have an ache. Sometimes during the year, like the toe injury, every time you step on that toe, put pressure on it, it\u2019s killing me. You kind of dread going on the floor.\u201d He recognized three months ago that his movements weren\u2019t as rapid as usual so he changed his diet and dropped 20 pounds. But that wasn\u2019t the strategic move that generated buzz within the Warriors organization. This was: Green hired Rich Paul, famously known as LeBron James\u2019 business partner, to represent him in future contract talks. The five-year, $82 million deal he signed in 2015 has one more year remaining. That puts the Warriors in a potentially urgent situation: Do they extend Green this summer or wait? If they allow Green to play next season without an extension, that could be an interesting scene; an emotionally charged player perhaps feeling unwanted and therefore making Kerr\u2019s life hell. It would make better sense to extend him this summer, or trade him. One or the other. But when the owner comes out publicly and uses the \u201cforever\u201d word, that removes an option for Bob Myers even if the general manager wanted to adopt the Branch Rickey model and dump a player a year too early than a year too late. So then it comes down to money. Green may feel he has been underpaid and is owed a somewhat significant raise, especially if the Warriors are unable to re-sign Durant and therefore have extra money to spend. Would it be a wise basketball decision to keep Green in the mix for another five years at a high price? How would the Warriors rebuild around Curry and Klay Thompson (assuming Thompson as expected re-signs this summer)? Championship teams have a tendency to fall in love with the players who helped them win championships \u2014 too much in love. Certainly, emotion was on Andre Iguodala\u2019s side when he signed an extension two years ago, and his production since has dropped considerably (although Iguodala\u2019s play this postseason has been solid). These teams are often, for lack of a better word, afraid to part with a popular player as he ages, partly because of fan and media backlash. And there\u2019s another reason for hesitating: Maybe the team will miss that player more than anyone thought, at least in the short term. At some point, the Warriors must address the elephant in the room, because the bill for these championships will come due. Yes, they\u2019re ready to move into a new San Francisco arena and revenue will flow, and the team will continue to snicker at the luxury tax. Kevin Durant\u2019s decision will be based on his whims, not money. The Warriors have no control over that. But they will have a significant say in Green\u2019s future. Or maybe the owner already spoke for them. * * * Shaun Powell has covered the NBA for more than 25 years. You can e-mail him  here , find his archive here  and follow him on  Twitter  . The Raptors\u2019 lineup \u2014 Kyle Lowry, Danny Green, Kawhi Leonard, Pascal Siakam and Marc Gasol \u2014 has outscored the Sixers by 25.1 points per 100 possessions in its 51 minutes. In 25 minutes with both starting lineups on the floor, the score has been Raptors 63, Sixers 46. Plus, those starters-vs.-starters minutes were even better for the Raptors in Game 2 ( 33-21 ) than they were in Game 1 ( 30-25 ). The 29.6 total minutes that the Raptors\u2019 starters played in Game 2 were the most minutes (by a pretty wide margin) that any lineup has played in any game in these playoffs. The only other lineup that has played more than 25 minutes in a game was the Portland starters ( 25.8 ) in Game 3 in Oklahoma City. The Raptors were a plus-13 in those 29.6 minutes, which means that they were outscored by 18 points in 18.4 minutes with at least one their reserves on the floor. (Random note when talking about how many minutes lineups have played in individual games: There have been 49 games in these playoffs through Wednesday and none of them have gone to overtime. Last year, four of 82 playoff games went to OT.) Some of that damage came against the Philadelphia starters. In 5.4 minutes with the Sixers\u2019 starting lineup on the floor against Toronto lineups that included at least one reserve, Philly outscored Toronto by seven points (12-5). But most of the damage came with reserves on the floor for both teams. In 13 minutes with at least one reserve on the floor for both Toronto and Philly, the Sixers outscored the Raptors by 11 points (31-20). The score of those reserves-on-the-floor-for-both-teams minutes in Game 1? Raptors 31, Sixers 20. Same score, different result. One difference in those minutes was that Sixers coach Brett Brown shortened his rotation and limited the time in which he had just one or two starters on the floor. In Game 1 (not counting garbage time), the Sixers were a minus-9 in 5.6 minutes with fewer than three starters on the floor. In Game 2, they were a minus-5 in just 1.4 minutes with fewer than three starters on the floor. Brown took Furkan Korkmaz out of the rotation, extended Ben Simmons\u2019 minutes ( from less than 34 in Game 1 to more than 44 in Game 2 ) and barely played a mostly-reserve unit that included James Ennis, Jonah Bolden and Boban Marjanovic. In 177 career games, the only time that Simmons has played more was the Sixers\u2019 triple-overtime game against Oklahoma City last season. The Raptors rank second in ball movement in the playoffs (352 passes per 24 minutes of possession), the only team that has averaged more passes per 24 than it did in the regular season. The other 15 playoff teams have averaged 30 fewer passes per 24 minutes of possession than they did in the regular season. The Raptors have averaged seven more. In their three games in which they\u2019ve passed the ball more than 350 times per 24, they\u2019ve scored just 104.1 points per 100 possessions. In Game 2, he had 68 touches , but passed it 38 times . The Sixers, with Simmons guarding him most of the time, did a better job of getting the ball out of Leonard\u2019s hands. Redick has been the primary defender on Green, as he was on Joe Harris in the first round. Harris (47.4 percent) was the league\u2019s leading 3-point shooter in the regular season and Green (45.5 percent) ranked second. Combined, Harris ( 4-for-21 ) and Green have shot 19 percent from 3-point range against the Sixers in these playoffs. On possessions in which Redick has been their defender, they\u2019re 0-for-9 from beyond the arc , having shot less often than they usually do, per Second Spectrum tracking. Redick is one of the best in the league at moving without the ball to get open. Over a century after hoops first appeared on the international stage in St. Louis, Mo., 3\u00d73 basketball will be featured at the Olympics for the first time in event history at the 2020 Olympics in Japan. On May 2, USA Basketball announced a multiyear partnership with Red Bull North America to build an elite pathway for men\u2019s and women\u2019s players who have a goal of competing at the highest levels of 3\u00d73 basketball. Through this partnership, Red Bull will host 23 qualifying tournaments across the country under the newly created Red Bull 3X series to identify the eight athletes who could potentially represent USA Basketball at the 2020 Olympics. The winning teams at these qualifiers will compete to accumulate enough individual FIBA points with the goal of moving on to the Red Bull USA Basketball 3X Regionals in the Fall of 2019, and then to the Red Bull USA Basketball 3X Nationals in March 2020, which will be used to select the USA men\u2019s and women\u2019s 3\u00d73 national teams. \u201cI think there\u2019s a lot of excitement because it creates an opportunity for an athlete that may not have been thinking that this is a pathway for them,\u201d said Jim Tooley, CEO of USA Basketball. \u201cIt opens up a door to a whole new generation of players that have an opportunity to wear U.S.A. across their chest and be a part of the Olympic games.\u201d The rules and regulations of 3\u00d73 basketball are different than the laws that international 5\u00d75 teams adhere to. The Red Bull 3X qualifiers, Red Bull USA Basketball 3X Nationals and the 2020 Summer Olympics 3\u00d73 competition will be played on a half court with a 10-minute game clock and 12-second shot clock, where the winner is first team to 21 points or the team in the lead at the end of the 10-minute regulation. Chris Mullin, a member of the 1992 USA Basketball \u201cDream Team\u201d and a 5-time NBA All-Star, is an ambassador for the sport of 3\u00d73 basketball and USA Basketball. The style of 3\u00d73 basketball and pace-of-play heavily intrigues the former Golden State Warrior and Indiana Pacer. This game here is fast-paced, up-tempo and probably more skill-based. There are different ways you can strategically approach it, but I think the level-playing field will appeal to so many more people,\u201d Mullin said. \u201cIf you played basketball at some point, every coach used 3\u00d73 as a teaching tool for conditioning or for skill development. 3\u00d73 is the purest form of basketball.\u201d Along with the chance to become the first crop of athletes to ever play 3\u00d73 basketball at the Olympic stage, USA Basketball participants will have the opportunity to represent their country at the international stage; something Chris Mullin said has never lost its effect on him. \u201cI go back to 1981 with my U.S.A. basketball experience and I have vivid memories of every experience, whether it was my coaches, my teammates or the places that they brought me all over the world,\u201d Mullin said. \u201cThe biggest impact in my basketball career has been through USA Basketball. Playing on your college team is great, playing on your NBA team is great, but USA Basketball is always a different experience. You\u2019re playing with different players in different countries and different coaches. To go through opening ceremonies, represent your country and potentially win a gold medal is pretty awesome.\u201d Along with Red Bull\u2019s impact on the qualifiers themselves, Red Bull Media House, an award-winning media company, will create original storytelling with USA Basketball to shine a light on the 3\u00d73 game, qualifiers and players, and celebrate the journey as the sport makes its debut at the Olympics. \u201cStorytelling is a big part of this process,\u201d Tooley said. \u201cI think it\u2019s important to make the public aware of this, more importantly the players, so that we can have a larger pool in helping us qualify for the Olympics. The qualifiers and the platform that Red Bull is providing here just amplifies the opportunity for each of these players and I think there\u2019ll be some amazing stories that come out of this whole journey.\u201d The first-ever Red Bull 3X qualifier will take place in Detroit, Michigan on June 22, 2019. This team that\u2019s been crafted around Lillard, the Blazers\u2019 All-NBA point guard, had to rescue their leader this time. \u201cDame Time\u201d never materialized Wednesday night at Pepsi Center in Game 2 of these Western Conference semifinals . Lillard was off, basically from the start. He simply didn\u2019t have his usual juice, shooting 5-for-17 from the floor and finishing with a postseason low 14 points and four assists. But the rest of the Blazers, from CJ McCollum to seldom-used reserve forward Jake Layman, showed up and carried the Blazers. That gave Portland a 97-90 win against the Denver Nuggets that evened the series as it heads to Portland for Friday\u2019s Game 3 at Moda Center ( 10:30 ET, ESPN ). \u201cI thought we wore them down in the first half, even though we weren\u2019t shooting the ball particularly well,\u201d Lillard said in assessing his team\u2019s thorough effort. \u201cThen in the second half it was just a grind it out kind of game. In those games you just have to be the tougher team. You have to want it more and then you have to have a few plays go your way.\u201d The thought of the Blazers stealing the home-court advantage on a night when Lillard struggled through one of his toughest games of the season would have seemed laughable before this. Doing it with Lillard struggling and down two other starters (starting center Jusuf Nurkic in late March; forward Maurice Harkless injured his ankle and played just 13 first-half minutes in Game 2) seemed \u2026 impossible? And yet there was McCollum, time after time, nailing a shot, grabbing a rebound or finding an open teammate to hold the Nuggets off late. He finished with a game-high 20 points (on 8-for-20 shooting, to go with six rebounds and six assists) on a physically bruising night when draining open shots turned out to be a difficult task for all involved. \u201cI was just trying to be aggressive,\u201d said McCollum, who averaged 33.7 points this season in games where Lillard was held to 15 points or less. \u201cI think that was the biggest thing, be aggressive, take what the defense gives me. Obviously, looking to score. But if it\u2019s not there, being aware of the weak side, being aware of the help defender and trying to make the right play.\u201d It worked that way for all of the Blazers, whatever it took to make the right plays. Al Farouq-Aminu bounced back from a rough Game 1, finishing with a double-double (11 points, 10 rebounds) and some tone-setting defense early on Nuggets power forward Paul Millsap. Enes Kanter, the starting center with the busted left shoulder, played Nuggets All-Star Nikola Jokic to a virtual standstill. In addition, Portland go some huge relief minutes from Zach Collins (10 points on 5-for-6 shooting, six rebounds). Seth Curry nailed a huge 3-pointer late and Evan Turner was right on time in his 18 minutes, providing quality work on both ends in a physical affair that left both teams spent physically and emotionally at the end. But perhaps the finest effort of the night was turned in by reserve swingman Rodney Hood, as his 15 points (on 5-for-11 shooting), three rebounds and three huge blocks couldn\u2019t have come at a better time. These Blazers are determined to write their ticket to the conference finals after being swept in the previous two postseason trips. Hood drilled a 22-footer from the corner off a sweet feed from Lillard with 2:02 to play, pushing the lead back to 10 (94-84) and silencing the crowd. He made three of four free throws in 17 seconds to finish things off. \u201cI thought CJ had a few big plays at the end,\u201d Lillard said. \u201cEnes had some big plays and Chief (Aminu) just competed in the paint with those big guys. Rodney in there competing, Enes competing on their offensive end. Even when they kept getting tips and second and third opportunities, they were having to work for it and we were getting shots contested down there, so we were able to get stops. In this type of game, I think that\u2019s what you need, a tough group effort. You just need a few guys to make a few big-time plays, and I thought C.J., Chief and Enes did that at the end of the game.\u201d How big was this win for the Blazers in the Lillard era? Consider that they are now 3-10 all-time now when Lillard has been held below 20 points. And they are just 3-18, dating back to 2014, in road playoff games. But they controlled this game early and snatched it from the team with the NBA\u2019s best regular-season home record (34-7). If the Blazers have visions of making the conference finals \u2014 and you better believe they do \u2014 this gutsy performance might pave the way. \u201cThey say the series starts when someone wins a game on the road and we were able to do that tonight,\u201d Lillard said. \u201cSo our goal is just to take it one game at a time and protect our home floor. We have two games on our home floor and we have to be just as sharp, if not better, because you know they are going to come back better. That\u2019s our main focus \u2014 just take care of our home floor.\u201d As Lillard says, there is still plenty of business to handle. The Nuggets earned the West\u2019s No. 2 seed for a reason. Denver pummeled Portland on the Game 2 boards with 23 offensive rebounds, but couldn\u2019t take advantage of those opportunities. They were 6-for-29 on 3-pointers and Jokic never got rolling like he did in Game 1. \u201cIt\u2019s not just how they play me,\u201d Jokic said. \u201cThey were aggressive as a team as a group. They were coming on my post-ups, but that doesn\u2019t really change how we play. The energy was kind of bad. I say that and we have 23 offensive rebounds. It was a weird game for us. They didn\u2019t even play that good, to be honest. They can play better than that. It was kind of a weird game where they were more aggressive than we were.\u201d It was a game where the Blazers clearly recognized the magnitude of the moment and rose to the challenge. Friday night becomes more than just a must-win game. It\u2019s an epic showdown, an opportunity for a team that\u2019s showcasing it\u2019s not all about Lillard to take serious control of this series and continue Portland\u2019s playoff redemption tour. * * * Sekou Smith is a veteran NBA reporter and NBA TV analyst.
