The teams are set to meet again this postseason, marking a rematch and the continuation of a rivalry between the team with the upper hand (Golden State) and the opponent (Houston) trying to oust their nemesis. Since 1984-85, there have been a lot of these rematch playoff series \u2014 and for the purposes of this exercise, we\u2019re excluding rematches in The Finals. Why? Because the Rockets are trying to reach The Finals for the first time since 1995. For a good number of teams, getting past that team keeping them from The Finals was a magical moment. In others, it was just good to vanquish a playoff demon. Other series were simply entertaining, even though they were over quickly. We went with \u201984-85 because it was Michael Jordan\u2019s rookie season, and he played a role in denying many a team and player a shot at their next goal. Here\u2019s a look at some great playoff rematches in NBA history since the 1985 playoffs. * * *  Why it gets a nod:  Boston won an East-high 57 games, but Detroit, which had pushed the Celtics to the limit in the East finals in 1987, won 54 games and the Central Division. General manager Jack McCloskey and coach Chuck Daly had surrounded 6-foot-1 star guard Isiah Thomas with rugged rebounders Bill Laimbeer and Rick Mahorn, scorers Adrian Dantley, Joe Dumars and Vinnie Johnson, and young, aggressive defensive forwards Dennis Rodman and John Salley. As for Boston, it boasted its core of Larry Bird, Kevin McHale, Robert Parish, Dennis Johnson and Danny Ainge as it sought a third straight NBA title. However, the Pistons bounced back from that \u201987 East finals loss with a vengeance. They won two of the first three games at the vaunted Boston Garden and closed out the Celtics in Game 6, reaching their first Finals in franchise history. The Pistons fell just short there, losing in Game 7 to the Los Angeles Lakers \u2014 but fittingly, reached The Finals again and swept the Lakers in the 1988 series. Why it gets a nod:  These teams didn\u2019t meet in a playoff series ever year from 1993 to 2000 \u2026 but it sure seemed that way. Overall, the teams met six times in that span, with the teams splitting those series (Indiana won in \u201995, \u201998 and 2000; New York won in \u201993, \u201994 and \u201999). Consider, too, that the winner of this series reached The Finals three times, including back-to-back matchups in the East finals (New York won in 1999; Indiana won in 2000). While the players on the periphery changed, the stars driving this series remained the same. For the Knicks, it was Patrick Ewing, John Starks, Charles Oakley, Derek Harper and Anthony Mason with Pat Riley (and later Jeff Van Gundy) on the sidelines. The Pacers countered with Reggie Miller, Mark Jackson, Rik Smits, Dale Davis and Derrick McKey\/Chris Mullin\/Jalen Rose with Larry Brown (and later Larry Bird) on the sidelines. The play in these series epitomized 1990s-era basketball and delivered countless playoff moments \u2014 Miller\u2019s eight points in 8.9 seconds, Ewing\u2019s jubilation in Game 7 of \u201994, Larry Johnson\u2019s four-point play \u2014 that still resonate with NBA fans today."},{"id":34109,"link":"https:\/\/www.nba.com\/news\/top-merchandise-jersey-sales-2018-19-season-official-release","date":"2019-04-25","title":"LeBron, Lakers top NBA's most popular merchandise lists","content":" NEW YORK \u2013  The National Basketball Association (NBA) and National Basketball Players Association (NBPA) today jointly announced that LeBron James and the Los Angeles Lakers secured the top spots on the NBA\u2019s Most Popular Jersey and Team Merchandise lists. It was that way in the 1960s and early \u201870s when he was known as \u201cMr. Clutch,\u201d and it is that way now, even at age 80. Jerry West cuts a figure you cannot easily forget, and not just because his silhouette inspired the NBA\u2019s logo. Sporting silver hair and a smile, West seems far from the brooding figure he was as a Hall of Fame combo guard for the Los Angeles Lakers \u2014 and later as a general manager (also most famously with the Lakers). He\u2019s pleasant and approachable before and after games. But when the ball goes up, West shows his trademark intensity while watching from courtside. Only West has had two legendary careers in the NBA. The highest honor any player could receive is to have their silhouette used as the league\u2019s logo. And more than a few GMs have remarked, regarding the annual Executive of the Year Award: They ought to name it after him. He has scaled back, to an extent and his official title with the LA Clippers is consultant. Yet his influence and fingerprints are all over a franchise about to embark on the most important summer since Steve Ballmer took over as owner six years ago. Ballmer\u2019s smartest decision was building a bond with Bill Gates. Doing so made him a billionaire 25 times over. Perhaps the next smartest move was hiring West, which may make Ballmer a champion. It all depends on what the Clippers \u2014 with West dropping knowledge in the back room \u2014 do with their money, assets, Draft picks and salary cap space this summer. And what does West say? \u201cWe know we can be a player.\u201d  But first, he will address the elephant in the room. Sort of, anyway. Because you\u2019ve asked, he hasn\u2019t heard from the Lakers about any possible return there after Magic Johnson\u2019s hasty resignation as team president . Yes, he obviously knows Jeanie Buss. Yes, there was an uncomfortable departure more than a decade ago because West lost a bitter power struggle with then-coach Phil Jackson (who also had a relationship with Jeanie Buss). Yes, she chose Magic, who had no experience, to run the Lakers instead of West. No, West insists he holds no resentment or grudges \u2014 the Lakers, after all, still employ one of his sons, Ryan. And no, he would not glance at the caller ID and just let the phone ring if Jeanie Buss did call. Yet, West isn\u2019t exactly waiting by the phone, either. \u201cI don\u2019t work there and I don\u2019t know what\u2019s going on over there,\u201d West said. He seems all-in with the Clippers, if only because they hired him quickly once his advisory role with the Warriors ended more than a year ago. West was somewhat surprised by the interest, yet it demonstrated how much respect there is for his opinions about personnel. Aside from his stellar front office work with the Lakers (which resulted in eight NBA titles), it was West who helped build the Memphis Grizzlies (after they relocated from Vancouver) and advised the Warriors not to trade Klay Thompson for Kevin Love . It was West who, fresh on the job, pushed the Clippers to trade Blake Griffin last season , which opened cap space. Naturally, West downplays his role. He says the Clippers are set in the front office with basketball ops president Lawrence Frank calling the shots (with help from Michael Winger and Trent Redden) and Doc Rivers as coach. He saves his biggest applause for Ballmer, though, and said the NBA\u2019s wealthiest owner will have the biggest sway this summer when the Clippers chase a superstar. \u201cI think everyone talks about his money when they speak about him,\u201d West said. \u201cI found him one of the most unique people I\u2019ve been around. He\u2019s a people person, a man of the people. It\u2019s a breath of fresh air to be around him. I\u2019ve been fortunate enough to be involved with some outstanding people and leaders who were really good, but this guy\u2019s at the top of the list. \u201cHe embraces the people and wants to learn and is great with our front office, with Lawrence and our coaches. I don\u2019t see any flaws with him as an owner. I wish all owners could be like him.\u201d West says Ballmer has created a culture that impacts every corner of the franchise. These aren\u2019t your father\u2019s Clippers, nor the one that employed West\u2019s close friend and former teammate, Elgin Baylor. \u201cWe have competitive people in our front office,\u201d West said. \u201cAnd Steve is as competitive as it comes. And that\u2019s why I\u2019m hopeful we can show (free agents) to look at what\u2019s been accomplished in a short time with him as an owner and a lot of the basketball people we have involved, along with Doc\u2019s resume, Lawrence\u2019s resume and how people are treated here.\u201d West gushes with praise over the job Rivers has done, too. He got 48 wins from a team that lacked an All-Star and shifted gears mid-season by trading top scorer Tobias Harris to the Philadelphia 76ers . \u201cDoc\u2019s been phenomenal,\u201d West said. \u201cHe\u2019s got a bunch of guys believing in themselves in order to win as many games as we did. I always try to predict before the season in my own mind how many games we can win, and I was off by some. Usually I\u2019m not off by very many. It has been amazing, really fun. \u201cWhen you see people competing and playing hard and believing in practice, it has an enormous amount of what happens during the games. I\u2019m thrilled for Doc. A season like this has to be very rewarding.\u201d The Clippers find themselves in an interesting position, as they could sign two free agents to near-max contracts. Already, they have a solid core in Lou Williams, Montrezl Harrell, Danilo Gallinari, Shai Gilgeous-Alexander, Landry Shamet and Pat Beverley (should he re-sign). Plus, of course, there are the charms of living and working in Los Angeles. Is this enough to get a Kawhi Leonard and\/or Kevin Durant and\/or Jimmy Butler and\/or Kemba Walker? The Clippers may never be in this great of a position again. West, however, throws caution to the wind.  \u201cCap space is fool\u2019s gold,\u201d he said. \u201cWe have to spend it wisely or not spend it. One of the two. \u201cAgain, it starts with ownership. A lot of people talk about free agency. You have to have a compelling reason for somebody to come to you. With the enormous amount of money that players have today obviously that\u2019s good, but the top free agents want more. We\u2019re obviously in the position to do something but we have to make a compelling case for people. It\u2019s nice to be one of the teams in that position, but we have to make a case going forward.\u201d The Clippers are sitting on a potential gold mine \u2026 if they manage it correctly. The Harris trade fetched the Miami Heat\u2019s unprotected No. 1 pick in 2021. That pick is projected to be very attractive because of the Heat\u2019s salary-cap situation and unknown desire to rebuild quickly. They also have the Sixers\u2019 2020 first-round pick (which is protected). Could those picks plus some Clippers players be used in potential trades (for maybe Anthony Davis)? \u201cWe have a lot of assets to work with, Draft picks that are very attractive,\u201d West said. In short, West is thrilled to part of a situation that could be franchise-changing. \u201cThis has been like a shot of adrenalin in my arm, to be involved with the people I\u2019m involved with,\u201d he said. \u201cEvery time I go over to the office, people are happy. Everyone is not there because they have a job. Everyone is here because they love it. Everyone kids each other and if you don\u2019t like to be kidded, don\u2019t come. It\u2019s fun. Everyone is having fun.\u201d While West could be salty or troll the Lakers and their perceived disorganization and dysfunction, he expresses the exact opposite sentiment for them. \u201cIt would be great if the Lakers were great and the Clippers were great,\u201d he said. \u201cWith the people they have over there, they\u2019re going to get a lot better. You see and hear a lot of negative things. People expect the Lakers to be up here because they have LeBron James. \u201cThere\u2019s so much more to this game and building a team. It\u2019s all about finding the people who compliment him best. Those young kids were probably looking at him in awe through the season and you certainly had no control over the injuries. With LeBron in the mix, anything\u2019s capable of happening. If they do something this summer, obviously it\u2019s going to be even better for them.\u201d But West also wants to make something very clear: \u201cWe want a better summer.\u201d * * * Shaun Powell has covered the NBA for more than 25 years. Taking matters into his own hands, Lillard scored a career playoff-high 50 points, including a 37-footer at the buzzer to eliminate the Oklahoma City Thunder from the playoffs in five games with a 118-115 victory . Amazingly, this wasn\u2019t the first time Lillard called series. He did the same thing to the Houston Rockets in 2014. Lillard was having a game for the ages even before the shot heard around the NBA. He played all 24 minutes of the first half, single-handedly keeping the Blazers close by scoring 34 points \u2014 the most by any player in one half in the postseason since Steve Nash in 2005. It was a signature performance for Lillard, capped by a buzzer-beater that will go down in history. Let\u2019s give it the Horry Scale treatment, shall we? A reminder on The Horry Scale: It breaks down a game-winning buzzer-beater (GWBB) in the categories of difficulty, game situation (was the team tied or behind at the time?), importance (playoff game or garden-variety night in November?) and celebration. Then we give it an overall grade on a scale of 1-5 Robert Horrys, named for the patron saint of last-second answered prayers. * * * DIFFICULTY: Lillard is no stranger to hitting big shots, but this was a 37-footer to clinch a playoff series. When it\u2019s Dame Time, Lillard has a way of making the most difficult shots look easy. GAME SITUATION: The Blazers erased a 15-point deficit in the fourth quarter and pulled even, 113-113, on CJ McCollum\u2019s 22-footer with 57 seconds left in regulation. Paul George and Lillard then exchanged buckets before Russell Westbrook missed a driving layup, which was corralled by Al-Farouq Aminu with 17.1 seconds left. Lillard took the handoff and calmly dribbled just inside of halfcourt near the Blazers logo, stared down Paul George, and then pulled up and hit the game-winner. CELEBRATION: The 3-pointer gave Lillard 50 points for the game and he waved good-bye to the Thunder bench amid a thunderous roar from the hometown crowd. He was promptly mobbed by his teammates as confetti rained down. As the Thunder left the floor, Lillard circled the court, high-fiving fans while the crowd chanted \u201cMVP! MVP!\u201d GRADE: It doesn\u2019t get any better than this. Five Horrys.  To say the Toronto Raptors and Philadelphia 76ers took care of business Tuesday night would be an understatement. Both teams started fast and crusied to series-clinching wins in their respective Game 5s, advancing to face each other in the Eastern Conference semifinals. Kyle Lowry set the tone for the Raptors, scoring Toronto\u2019s first nine points. Meanwhile, the Orlando Magic missed 10 of their first 11 shots and went 0 for 7 from 3-point range in the first. The Raptors jumped out to a 24-7 lead and never looked back in their 115-96 victory . Kawhi Leonard had another ruthlessly efficient game, scoring 27 points on 8-for-11 shooting, including 5 of 5 from 3-point range. He checked out to cheers of \u201cMVP, MVP\u201d with 8:05 to play and Toronto up by 30. The Sixers faced even less resistance from the Brooklyn Nets, who looked overmatched since winning the series opener. Joel Embiid again shook off a bad left knee and had six points and five rebounds in the first two minutes. The Sixers scored the first 14 points of the game, and led 30-6 with two minutes left in the first quarter. \u201cThey did whatever they wanted before we could even get on the board,\u201d Nets All-Star guard D\u2019Angelo Russell said. Embiid finished with 23 points and 13 rebounds in just 20 minutes, and no starter played more than 27 minutes in the 122-100 romp . If there\u2019s one play that summed up this game, it\u2019s Embiid\u2019s baseline jam over Rondae Hollis-Jefferson. So we finally get another Sixers-Raptors series. It\u2019s been 18 years since Philadelphia and Tornoto met in the playoffs, when Allen Iverson\u2019s 76ers defeated the Vince Carter-led Raptors in seven games. Speculation about Washington\u2019s availability ran rampant, but Wildcat fans were encouraged after he posted a meme on Twitter that clearly showed he had shed the cast. Washington has a complete package. He uses his 7-foot-3 wingspan to full advantage, blocking shots or launching a deadly jump hook. After shooting 23.8 from 3-point range as a freshman and taking just 21 shots from behind the arc, he became a legitimate threat (41.9, 31-for-47). He\u2019s has the patience to see plays develop and affect them with his passing ability. Washington, again taking advantage of his length, can also defend multiple positions. After Washington racked up 20 points and 13 rebounds against Kansas in a late-January game in the Big 12-SEC Challenge, Calipari raved about the sophomore\u2019s energy. Cal\u2019s job isn\u2019t necessarily easier because he recruits so many five-star freshmen. The Portland Trail Blazers\u2019 star put on a dazzling display Sunday night at Chesapeake Energy Arena, teaching a masterclass while taking on one of the league\u2019s other elite point guards: Oklahoma City\u2019s former Kia MVP and triple-double machine, Russell Westbrook. Lillard staked the Blazers to a sizable third-quarter cushion, one that expanded to as many as 19 points. He manipulated the entire floor down the stretch and helped his crew exit the building with a momentum-stealing 111-98 victory and a 3-1 lead in this series. It was the sort of performance Lillard hinted at a day earlier, when the Thunder were still riding high from a Game 3 win where they taunted and tweaked the Trail Blazers, using every tool they could to make a dent in this series. Lillard swore he wouldn\u2019t allow his team to fall into the trap of trading taunts. There would be no retaliation for Paul George\u2019s Game 3 reverse dunk that didn\u2019t beat the final buzzer when the game was already well in hand. There was no need to respond to Westbrook\u2019s gestures and chatter after seemingly every basket. He stuck to his word. There was too much at stake in Game 4 for the focus to be anywhere and, in doing so, the Blazers snapped an eight-game road playoff losing streak dating to 2016. Lillard and the Blazers, still smarting from last spring\u2019s sweep at the hands of the No. 6-seeded New Orleans Pelicans, can ill afford to play petty games with so much riding on this postseason. They stumbled down the stretch of the regular season, dealing with injuries to CJ McCollum and then the devastating loss of their starting center, Jusuf Nurkic, on March 25 . Portland\u2019s margin for error this spring is razor thin, what with all the doubters wondering if they\u2019d fall victim in the first round again after earning the No. 3 seed in back-to-back playoffs. \u201cWhen we had our backs against the wall dealing with those injuries, we trusted each other, we leaned on each other more,\u201d said Lillard after roasting Westbrook and the Thunder for 24 points and eight assists. \u201cIt just made us better in the long run, instead of us saying \u2018We got some injuries, Dame you gotta average 35 and do all this extra stuff,\u2019 instead of that the mentality was, \u2018Let\u2019s keep the ball moving, make the right plays defensively and let\u2019s just be a better unit.\u2019 Instead of turning to me or somebody else to score a bunch of points. We just made it a group thing and I think that was for our team. \u201cAnd as far as people doubting us coming in, you don\u2019t just become the third seed luckily, especially two times in a row. Last year our performance wasn\u2019t great in the playoffs. But I think we showed a lot by backing it up this year and getting the third seed again. We don\u2019t focus on doubters. We focus on ourselves. We accept the responsibility for our past two playoff performances and we\u2019ve moved on. And I think that\u2019s one of the biggest and best things we did for ourselves is we did not hold on to it. We moved on and we have a better run now. We just have to finish it.\u201d They have an opportunity to finish this series at home Tuesday night because they\u2019ve learned to move on immediately. Their appetite for all of the \u201cperiphery\u201d antics, as Blazers coach Terry Stotts categorized all of the back and forth from the first three games of the series, was nonexistent. Steady had to be the rule. \u201cThis was the chance they would have had to tie the series up,\u201d Lillard said. \u201cSo you expect the excitement, you expect the passion. But after last game it was a big deal being made out of the back and forth and we weren\u2019t emotional about it. We were just competing and passionate about the game as well. \u201cBut it\u2019s like I said earlier. We didn\u2019t really engage in it because our focus was our team. Like I told you guys, we\u2019re not going go out here and go crazy on the refs. We\u2019re not going go out here and get in shouting matches and all that stuff. We\u2019re going go out here do whatever gives us a chance to win games. And I was proud how our team stuck to that.\u201d While Westbrook played a strange, disengaged game at such a crucial time (5-for-21 shooting, missing his last 10 shots), Lillard was plugged in from the start. He probed and studied OKC\u2019s defense until he felt it was the right time to strike. It was \u201cDame Time\u201d in the third quarter, like it was in Game 3. Lillard scored 15 points \u2014 including three straight back-breaking deep 3-pointers \u2014 during a wicked run that saw the Blazers build that 19-point lead. Even after a rough 2-for-8 shooting start in the first half Sunday night, Lillard said he relished the position his team was in. \u201cI loved it, I think anytime we can be on the road playing against a really good team and not playing well and I haven\u2019t been scoring the ball and we have a lead that late in the game that\u2019s a great situation,\u201d Lillard said. \u201cJust because a lot of time I\u2019m not in that position. I\u2019m usually getting attempts, scoring the ball and that\u2019s what\u2019s working for us. Tonight it wasn\u2019t like that. But I also know that all I need is to see it go in one time and once it goes in that can turn into three or four or five. That\u2019s kind if what happened in the third quarter.\u201d The Blazers know what happens when Lillard taps his arm, pointing at the imaginary watch that signals his time to shine. \u201cWell, we\u2019ve kind of come to expect it,\u201d Blazers coach Terry Stotts said. \u201cI think he really manages the game. I think he senses the moment when we need him to do different things. He\u2019s an ultimate competitor. However the game is going, if it\u2019s going well, if it\u2019s not going so well, he\u2019s going to give it his all. A lot of times that\u2019s with his scoring. But I think his leadership, his passing, the way he directs the team, the example he sets, all that.\u201d \u201cAll that\u201d was on display when the Blazers needed it most in Game 4. Lillard was also the counselor, floor general and calming presence needed to handle this delicate business. He calmed down young big man Zach Collins to keep him from getting a technical foul for jawing with an official. He repeatedly found Al-Farouq Aminu (19 points, 4-for-9 on 3-pointers) for quality shots. When the Thunder tightened up their defense on Lillard, he deferred to McCollum (team-high 27 points) to beat back OKC\u2019s late rally. \u201cI think we\u2019re a lot more mature than we\u2019ve been in past years,\u201d said McCollum, who closed the Thunder out late with 13 points in the fourth quarter. \u201cWe can handle adversity, understanding what we wanted to accomplish tonight \u2026 multiple times we had discussions about the fact that if they don\u2019t have a black-and-red jersey on, don\u2019t talk to them. Don\u2019t talk to the refs, don\u2019t talk to the other team. Execute our game plan, and let\u2019s get out of here with a win.\u201d That shared mindset and leadership style from Lillard and McCollum is what drives these Blazers. It\u2019s what will allow them to advance in this postseason and what will guide them in the future. When the pair embraced on the floor as they subbed out late in Sunday\u2019s game, there was an acknowledgement of all they\u2019ve been through \u2026 and could envision as well. \u201cWe\u2019ve dealt with a lot of criticism and lot of praise and we were just having a good conversation that was private, so I won\u2019t share with you guys,\u201d McCollum said. \u201cBut we were just talking about what we can accomplish going forward and what we\u2019ve accomplished so far and just the fact that we got love for each other.\u201d * * * Sekou Smith is a veteran NBA reporter and NBA TV analyst. Saturday in Salt Lake City, James Harden missed his first 15 shots. Sunday afternoon in Los Angeles, Stephen Curry doinked all but one of his nine 3-point attempts . Did someone misalign their moons? Curry nearly drowned in misfires, no matter how open he was left by the LA Clippers. Yet, just as Harden\u2019s team discovered a night earlier, it didn\u2019t alter the eventual outcome. Yes, ultimately it was all about the Clippers falling even if Curry\u2019s shots didn\u2019t. That means all was well with the Warriors after a 113-105 Game 4 victory that moved them closer to an anticipated Western Conference semifinals series with Harden and the Houston Rockets. This is the luxury the Warriors enjoy, that even an off-night by one All-Star \u2014 or All-NBA \u2014 player can be compartmentalized by others, in this case Kevin Durant (33 points) and Klay Thompson (32). But this is nothing new for the Warriors in this dynastic era, where \u201cStrength In Numbers\u201d is a team slogan (and bad news for the rest of the league). Curry watched the Rockets-Jazz game, in which Harden, the league\u2019s leading regular-season scorer at 36.3 ppg, couldn\u2019t break through until the final quarter . The Rockets astonishingly kept the game close before the basketball gods finally bestowed mercy on Harden and released the handcuffs on his wrists. He and the Rockets are now one win from moving on. \u201cI like what [Harden] said afterward \u2014 it\u2019s all about winning,\u201d Curry said. \u201cWhether you have it going or not, whether you see the ball going in or not, you still have to stay engaged and obviously this is a team game. You have to have everyone contribute. You really don\u2019t care about who\u2019s night it is.\u201d That sounds nice in theory and though there is some truth, the reality is only a handful of NBA teams can play this \u201cteam game\u201d of which Curry speaks about, these few who are blessed with multiple stars who can cover for each other. How many can watch their franchise player go 3-for-14, as Curry did Sunday, then yawn and simply throw the ball to Durant? Or Thompson? Again, this is what makes the Warriors the Warriors. While his fellow Splash Brother was busy building homes, Thompson ripped through the poor Clippers with 27 in the first half, making his first seven shots. Then Durant picked his spots to chip in, and suddenly the level of angst dropped considerably. Curry had a good feeling about Thompson when he saw his teammate return to the team hotel Saturday in typical Thompson fashion. \u201cHe walked into the hotel with a wet T-shirt with his shades on,\u201d Curry said. \u201cTypical Klay vibe. I knew what that meant. Nice for him to show out like he did. We needed every bit of it.\u201d If you survey the playoff landscape, you wonder if anyone can offset this many weapons in the fight. How about the Bucks and their singular force, Giannis Antetokoumnpo? Can they win a game, or even two, if he disappears offensively? How about the Philadelphia 76ers or the Toronto Raptors or the Boston Celtics going far with their top gun going cold? Should the Warriors and Rockets meet, we\u2019re set for an interesting scenario over the weekend. Because of the considerable gap between Harden\u2019s ability to get buckets and the next leading scorer on the Rockets, Chris Paul, the Rockets will lean heavily on Harden to drop at least 30, or else. The Warriors have no such worry unless two stars are suffering in the same game, and how unusual would that be, if, say Durant and Curry are brothers in misery? Even if one of them goes cold, what are the chances of it happening to that same player in the same series, or even the next game? As Warriors coach Steve Kerr said: \u201cGuys sometimes have a huge night, sometimes they struggle, but the great players like Steph, Kevin and Klay, they bounce back pretty quickly from tough shooting nights.\u201d Don\u2019t be surprised if Curry closes out the Clippers with a ballistic Game 5 on Wednesday. Matter of fact, be shocked if it doesn\u2019t happen, considering his history and his pattern. \u201cIt\u2019s basketball, you can\u2019t get too high or low,\u201d Curry said. \u201cThe numbers don\u2019t look good but it\u2019s timing and making the right decisions and being a threat until something opens up. You like to have a game where things might not be going your way but you\u2019re still impacting the game in some way or another.\u201d True enough, because Curry is much more than a shooter; his court vision can bail him out when the shot\u2019s not falling. Curry racked up seven assists and also added 10 rebounds Sunday, a productive night despite the missed shots. \u201cYou can\u2019t get down and let that energy affect you,\u201d he said. \u201cYou have to make some tough shots. I got 14 shots and 11 were solid ones. I forced up three of them and I know that because I think about those positions and I want them back. You just try to be as patient as possible, knowing that it\u2019ll come around eventually. You still have to be a threat out there, be aggressive and stand out on the floor and make the right plays. Play great defense, not turn the ball over, just play hard.\u201d Curry will be fine and the Warriors didn\u2019t miss his shot much on Sunday, anyway. It helped that the Clippers, bless their hard-working souls, lack the superstar needed to take advantage of these situations in the playoffs. All roads point to the second round and another round with the Houston team that took the Warriors to the seven-game limit last spring. It\u2019s a series where the Rockets might pay the price this time if Harden rolls another 3-for-20. * * * Shaun Powell has covered the NBA for more than 25 years. You can e-mail him  here  . "},{"id":34130,"link":"https:\/\/www.nba.com\/news\/nets-general-mananger-sean-marks-fined-suspended","date":"2019-04-21","title":"Nets GM fined, suspended for entering refs locker room","content":" NEW YORK (AP) \u2014 Nets general manager Sean Marks has been suspended one game without pay and fined $25,000 for entering the referees\u2019 locker room after Brooklyn\u2019s loss to Philadelphia in Game 4 of their playoff series. Marks will serve his suspension Tuesday, when the 76ers try to wrap up the series in Game 5 in Philadelphia."},{"id":34159,"link":"https:\/\/www.nba.com\/news\/curtis-jones-warriors-security-guard-curry-shot-qa","date":"2019-04-21","title":"Q&A: Warriors' security guard on Curry's tunnel shot","content":" There is no pregame ritual quite like the one executed by Stephen Curry, the two-time MVP guard whose shooting display has become must-see. When the doors open at Oracle Arena, hundreds of fans make a mad rush to the lower seats near the north basket to catch a glimpse of Curry in action before he takes the court roughly an hour later at tipoff. This is a common scene not only at Oracle but wherever the Warriors play. Yet only at Oracle are the fans blessed with a closing treat \u2014 Curry heaving 40-foot 3-pointers from off the court and out of view. In what is now famously known as the Tunnel Shot, Curry retreats beyond the mouth of the tunnel used by the Warriors when they enter the game and takes three or four shots from the tunnel. Often, he\u2019ll make at least one, and rarely does Curry go more than two games without splashing. What began as a playful ending to his pregame warmup is now a part of the Curry lore \u2014 and a popular one, too. In addition to being enjoyed by the fans, the Tunnel Shot has been shown on countless TV and Internet highlights and written up in newspapers worldwide. There is a co-star to this show. He is Curtis Jones, a 67-year-old grandfather who works security at Oracle. Jones has the responsibility of throwing passes to Curry for the Tunnel Shot, which he does underhanded from about 15 feet away and with amazing aim and a fierce determination to get each pass right where Curry likes it. Therefore, Jones has become an unlikely side player in the Warriors\u2019 dynasty as well as a staple at the oldest arena in the NBA, which will be permanently vacated once the Warriors\u2019 season ends. Shaun Powell spoke with Jones about Curry, the shot, the arena and what may become the end to an unexpected dream.  Shaun Powell: Why has the Tunnel Shot become so popular? Curtis Jones: It\u2019s because of the person doing the shooting and the difficulty of the shot. A lot of people have seen Steph take unbelievable shots during games, I mean crazy shots. But this shot might be the most amazing. He\u2019s not on the court. He\u2019s at a weird angle. He can\u2019t get a lucky bounce off the rim. And most people can\u2019t even see him release the shot. Plus, I imagine he might be a little tired from the warmup. But he takes it seriously and so I take it seriously. That\u2019s my nature anyway when I\u2019m doing my job. It\u2019s also fun and a lot of people enjoy it. I get a kick out of it, too. SP: How did this get started? CJ: One day Steph was shooting around and my security post is right at the tunnel entrance and so he always passes me on the way to the court and the way off the court. So I kind of inquired about his shot. All of a sudden, and I didn\u2019t know he was going to do this, he threw the ball to me and asked for it back. So I threw it back and he took the shot form the tunnel. It kind of like became a ritual as he would go out and warm up. After he would leave the court, he would tap me on my shoulder and it got to a point where it was understood what I was supposed to do from that point on. SP: Just like that? CJ: Yeah. It was a way of us connecting and if you know Steph, he connects with people naturally and very easily; that\u2019s why so many fans like him. Anyway, Steph said he got the idea for the shot from Monta Ellis, his former teammate, but from what I understand Monta, took the shot after practice and I don\u2019t go to practices. I don\u2019t recall Monta taking it before a game. Maybe he did and I didn\u2019t notice. SP: What\u2019s the best Steph has ever done with the shot? CJ : He made five in a row. SP: How did you get the job? CJ: I started here in 1994 after I got a tip from a friend of mine that the arena was hiring. I just came down, filled out an application and got a call later on to come to orientation. I\u2019ve been working in the building for 25 years and working on the floor for 21 years. SP: Did you play basketball? CJ: Not anywhere near this level. I\u2019m from Monroe, Louisiana. We had a basketball rim in our back yard. We ended up breaking it and replacing it with a basket tied to a tree. SP: But you seem so natural with the ball in your hands and especially making those passes to Steph. CJ: I always liked to pass when I did play as a kid. I developed strong hands and I could always get the ball to the right spot. I also learned how to get a good grip on the ball because when we played, we played either in the street or on the grass, and sometimes the ball would take a funny bounce. You had to grab it to keep your dribble. Who knew that this would help me many years later? It\u2019s a blessing. You just never know. SP: I noticed that when you came here today, you grabbed a ball from the rack and placed it underneath the courtside seats. Why? CJ : Well, I just don\u2019t want to throw any old ball to Steph. It has to be the right ball, not too old, not too new and slick. Something that would give him great rotation. So I look for the perfect ball as soon as I walk in the door and well before Steph comes out. Once I find it, I put it away so it\u2019ll be fresh for him, nobody else\u2019s hands on it. I put it under the seat and then put some chairs around in so nobody will notice it. SP: What if he misses the shot? Do you use another ball from the rack? CJ: No, we\u2019ll have someone get that same ball and then give it back to me. Nobody throws the ball to Steph but me. I don\u2019t think Steph wants it any other way. SP : How would you describe the atmosphere from the crowds? CJ : Funny thing is, we didn\u2019t have any crowds the first couple of months, the crowds were very sparse, then all of a sudden people started to come and get into position. It became a big deal. It\u2019s come to the point where everyone understands to get here as soon as they can and stay in their place. SP : Does it get out of hand? CJ : Not really. The fans are really under control and so there\u2019s no real pushing and shoving and people getting into disagreements. They just want to see the shot, and there\u2019s no bad seat in the house. SP : So many people are bending over the railing to get a piece of Steph. How does he handle that? CJ : Well, he can\u2019t really stop and do something for everyone. It bothers me to see a child disappointed and hurt. Curry means that much to them. Even a high five, a kid will remember that for the rest of his life. And he does as much as he can. But every now and then we\u2019ll get a kid who\u2019s challenged in a way, maybe in a wheelchair, or someone from Make A Wish. And someone will let me know, so when Steph comes off the court, I\u2019ll make sure that kid gets some time with him. He talks to them as if everything is OK. He doesn\u2019t waver. It\u2019s a natural reaction. He walks over and greets him like I\u2019m speaking to you right now. I respect him for that. He has a gift for that. SP : Have you and Steph had any meaningful conversation? CJ: You know, not really. We\u2019ve exchanged a few words every now and then and he\u2019s always very happy to see me. The only time I see him is when he\u2019s coming on the court or coming off the court and there\u2019s no time to talk. Besides, I have to keep my concentration at my post, because there\u2019s always people around and I have to constantly check their credentials and make sure nobody gets on the court that doesn\u2019t belong. SP: What would you two talk about? CJ : It would be a normal conversation because I\u2019m a down to earth person and he\u2019s a down to earth person. We\u2019d probably talk about his kids, how\u2019s his family. SP: You realize you\u2019re a celebrity, right? CJ : (Laughs) I guess I am, although I really don\u2019t carry myself that way. It was amazing to see myself on TV for the first time. And my family, they\u2019re more excited than I am. I have a nephew in Delaware who saw me and he told all his friends and they got excited. I\u2019m kind of humble, actually. SP: Oracle Arena is closing the doors as soon as the season\u2019s over. The Warriors are moving to Chase Center in San Francisco and depending on the configuration of the floor, the Tunnel Shot might become extinct or impossible. Anyway, are you moving there as well? CJ : We haven\u2019t really had that discussion about that. I\u2019d like to go but I don\u2019t know. I\u2019d go. I\u2019m retired. I don\u2019t want to sit down in my life. I want to be active. If I\u2019m not part of it any longer, I guess I\u2019ll stay here and work whatever events they\u2019ll have here. That would be bittersweet. After you do something for so long you get accustomed to it and enjoy it. In life you look for positive things to enjoy so you\u2019ll have great memories as you grow older. And I\u2019m getting older. * * * Shaun Powell has covered the NBA for more than 25 years. You can e-mail him  here  . For only one of the 256 could you say the following: His team was at least five points per 100 possessions better offensively and at least five points per 100 possessions better defensively with him on the floor than they were with him off the floor. That player was Joel Embiid . The additions of Jimmy Butler and Tobias Harris this season have given the Sixers a ridiculously talented starting lineup. And with Embiid missing Game 3 of their first-round series against the Brooklyn Nets on Thursday, the rest of that lineup took care of business. But Embiid remains the Sixers\u2019 \u201ccrown jewel,\u201d as head coach Brett Brown has called him. He\u2019s a matchup problem for every team in the league and the difference maker for the Sixers on both ends of the floor. In Game 4 on Saturday, the Nets just couldn\u2019t match up, Embiid was the difference, and the Sixers came away with a 112-108 victory to take a 3-1 series lead. Embiid\u2019s line: 31 points, 16 rebounds, seven assists, six blocks and two steals in less than 32 minutes. He was the fulcrum on both ends of the floor. The Nets were one of five teams that took more than 40 percent of their shots from 3-point range this season. But they also led the league with 30.9 points per game scored on drives. The Nets\u2019 biggest offensive weapons in this series have been Caris LeVert and Spencer Dinwiddie, guards who can attack the rim by exploiting the Sixers\u2019 lack of quickness on the perimeter. Brooklyn was successful at the rim against Embiid on a few occasions, with both LeVert and Dinwiddie scoring over him. He also had a close-up look at a Jarrett Allen dunk when LeVert drove at him and dished off. But while the Philadelphia defense did bend at times, it never broke when Embiid was on the floor. And when Jared Dudley was ejected for shoving Embiid early in the third quarter, Brooklyn lost the only player who could possibly play center and make Embiid pay for hanging near the rim. \u201cI know these guys are going to go at me, because they want me to retaliate,\u201d Embiid said about the skirmish that started when he hit Allen with what was deemed a flagrant foul. \u201cSo I got to be the mature one on the court and just stay cool and don\u2019t react. I could have reacted, but I felt like my team needed me more than they did Jared Dudley. I just got to stay cool and mature and do my job.\u201d On defense, his job was to help the Sixers get stops. And with Embiid on the floor, Brooklyn scored just 62 points on 71 possessions. With him off the floor, the Nets scored 46 points on just 33 possessions. On the other end, Brooklyn just couldn\u2019t deal with Embiid in the paint. When they tried to front him in the post, the Sixers flashed a man to the foul line and got the ball to Embiid with high-low action. When he was doubled in the post, he found the open man, often Ben Simmons cutting to the rim after the Nets sent help off him. When Embiid caught the ball on the perimeter, he didn\u2019t settle, instead backing down into the post where he could score more easily or draw an extra defender (or two). In this series, Embiid has shot 22-for-35 (63 percent) in the paint and 3-for-14 (21 percent) outside it. \u201cI\u2019m just trying to live in the paint,\u201d he said. \u201cThey\u2019re going to have to double-team me. I figured that. They\u2019re going to have to send two or three guys. If they\u2019re going to guard me in single coverage, I\u2019m going to dominate. And then I\u2019m also going to make the right pass. That\u2019s my job. We found it.\u201d With the Sixers down one with 25 seconds left in the game, the Sixers looked to Embiid, with Allen fronting him in the post. Joe Harris crashed from the weak side and knocked the ball out of Embiid\u2019s hands, but Embiid beat Traveon Graham to the ball and, with one hand, got the ball to an open Mike Scott (Harris\u2019 man) in the corner for what turned out to be the game-winning 3-pointer with 18.6 seconds left. The goal in NBA offense is to find an advantage and use it to exploit the defense and get an open shot. Embiid is that advantage for the Sixers whenever he walks on the floor. And with him on the floor on Saturday, the Sixers scored 80 points on 69 possessions. With him off the floor, they scored just 32 points on 35 possessions. \u201cHe was dominant,\u201d Brown said. \u201cThere were times you can see that it\u2019s still raw, and there are some decisions that he probably would like to have over again. But given the volume of playing time lately that he hasn\u2019t had, it\u2019s just a dominant performance. What more can you say?\u201d Embiid remains the most important piece to the Sixers\u2019 puzzle. Really, when it comes to competing with the best teams in the league, he is the puzzle. The question is just how much they\u2019re going to have him going forward. For every game in this series, Brown hasn\u2019t known until shortly before tip-off whether or not he\u2019s going to have his most important player. Embiid was clearly hobbled in Game 1, better in Game 2, absent in Game 3, and dominant in Game 4. Maybe things are trending in the right direction. And maybe Embiid\u2019s status will remain a question from here on out. The Sixers\u2019 ability to compete for an Eastern Conference championship hangs in the balance. * * * John Schuhmann is a senior stats analyst for NBA.com. Who knew he\u2019d actually need one here in the playoffs? Life comes at you fast, and so does opportunity, and suddenly Bogut has gone from insurance policy to rotational player and perhaps the Warriors\u2019 best big man, all in a matter of days. \u201cIt\u2019s crazy,\u201d he said in that familiar Aussie twang. Two months ago, Bogut was in his native country, having just wrapped up an MVP season in Australia\u2019s National Basketball League when his caller ID showed a familiar area code from the U.S. On Sunday, he\u2019ll get some significant burn at Staples Center when the Warriors try to push their first-round lead over the Clippers toward 3-1. This is a case of a player being in the right place at the right time and a two-time defending champion not taking their place in the league for granted. As a result, Bogut has found new life for his NBA career at age 34 and the Warriors, a capable replacement for starter DeMarcus Cousins, who\u2019s probably done for the season with a torn right quad. \u201cAndrew has been a godsend,\u201d said coach Steve Kerr. \u201cThe reason it was such a great signing for us, is not that he\u2019s just a hell of a player, but because he has been with us and he is so familiar with what we do.\u201d Yes, this is a remarriage of convenience \u2014 Bogut knows the system and his way around the locker room, and the Warriors know what he brings and how to squeeze him into the system. When they signed him in mid-March, the plan was for Bogut to back up Cousins but mostly be someone who could provide additional leadership in the locker room and a wise voice on the bench. The bonus is Bogut is healthy, in tremendous shape and moving smoothly on the floor. This is the pleasant surprise the Warriors weren\u2019t fully expecting, but will take, even though the center position has never been emphasized during the Kerr era. Bogut\u2019s play in Game 3 made the Warriors more confident and reassured about the 5, anyway. He started, grabbed 14 rebounds, found open teammates and dished out five assists, proving respectable while defending the rim. Bogut played 25 minutes, his most in the NBA since January of 2017. \u201cHe looks amazing,\u201d Draymond Green said. \u201cIncredible to have him back where he belongs.\u201d Bogut has a bit of unfinished business with the Warriors. During his previous tour of duty, he suffered a knee injury in Game 5 and was lost for the remainder of the 2016 NBA Finals, which the Warriors ultimately lost to Cleveland. Bogut was a factor in that series and his injury, along with Green\u2019s infamous one-game suspension, probably cost Bogut a second championship ring and the Warriors a potential fourth during this ongoing dynastic run. The former No. 1 overall pick closed out his first 13-year stint in the NBA as a drifter, seeing scattered playing time with the Mavericks, Cavaliers and Lakers after the Warriors didn\u2019t re-sign him, just 51 games total. When the Lakers released him after 24 games in 2017, there were no other takers and Bogut retreated to Australia, all but retired from the NBA. Once his body healed, he chose to return to the game to success with the Sydney Kings. He was league MVP and Defensive Player of the Year, averaging 11.4 points, 11.6 rebounds, 3.4 assists and 2.7 blocks in 30 games. Besides basketball, Bogut began raising a family and the balance was beneficial to him. \u201cI feel good,\u201d Bogut said. \u201cProbably more mentally as well. Just felt burnt out mentally with the grind of the NBA season for 13 seasons straight. It was good to just get home and get back to some normality, playing in Australia once or twice a week. And then obviously having a family now. Family kind of changes my perspective of what should be stressful and what shouldn\u2019t be and what\u2019s important in life.\u201d The urge to return to the States simmered inside him, however, especially since he wouldn\u2019t need to trudge through an entire NBA season. Bogut says that\u2019s unlikely to happen again, no matter how well he plays from here. Still, he wants to make the most of the next two months, assuming the Warriors are still in business. \u201cLeaving and coming back makes you appreciate this much more,\u201d he said. \u201cHaving another opportunity to play with the best players in the world is great. I probably appreciate it much more now. When you\u2019re stuck in the moment as I was the first time around, I don\u2019t think I enjoyed it and been appreciative of it as I should\u2019ve been.\u201d From a numbers standpoint, the Warriors didn\u2019t need Bogut, even though Damian Jones, the starting center at the season\u2019s onset, is out with a torn pectoral muscle. Not only was Cousins healthy, but Kevon Looney showed growth and a new confidence in his mid-range shot. Looney, however, is more of a power forward, and the Warriors had nothing to lose by bringing in Bogut, a favorite of Kerr thanks to his instincts and intelligence. \u201cAndrew is a brilliant basketball mind,\u201d Kerr said. \u201cHe\u2019s one of those guys when you call a play or teach him a play, he gets it right away. He understands where all five players should be and he understands why you\u2019re running the play. He just has beautiful feel for the game. He sees and feels the game like Andre (Iguodala) does, only at the center position. His voice is so prominent, when you\u2019re down on the floor and our team is on defense, you can hear Boges barking out commands, barking out signals, letting guys know where they need to be, like he \u2019s like a quarterback out there.\u201d While Cousins was obviously a productive player, he often struggled in the pick and roll and has never been known for defense. Those are areas of strength for Bogut and it gives Kerr more options with Steph Curry and Klay Thompson, shooters who are deadly when sprung off picks. Also, with the emphasis on half-court play in the playoffs, Bogut\u2019s 7-foot stature becomes an asset near the rim on both ends. \u201cThis is the reason we signed him,\u201d Curry said. \u201cWe never wanted to talk about it and want to admit of the possibility. But it was to shore up that center position in case of injury. It\u2019s how it has played out. We have to be ready to adjust and win basketball games.\u201d The Clippers don\u2019t offer much resistance in the middle. Yet if the playoff seeds hold true and the Warriors see the Rockets in the second round, Bogut will be valuable against Clint Capela, who is active offensively in the pick and roll. \u201cI just want to be a positive to the team,\u201d Bogut said. \u201cThey don\u2019t need me to score, obviously. But whatever they need, really. I can be a voice defensively, control the paint and do what they need me to do. I feel I fit in pretty well. Not a whole lot has changed in the system and the flow of the game since my first time here. Hopefully as an older guy who\u2019s played around the world I can use my voice a little more and get guys going.\u201d One month into this reunion, Bogut and the Warriors have discovered they need each other more than either imagined. * * * Shaun Powell has covered the NBA for more than 25 years. "},{"id":34231,"link":"https:\/\/www.nba.com\/news\/pat-connaughton-milwaukee-bucks-qa","date":"2019-04-20","title":"Q&A: Pat Connaughton enjoying the NBA lifestyle while keeping MLB option open","content":" The game had been decided for a while when Pat Connaughton, a Milwaukee reserve guard, got Detroit\u2019s Langston Galloway on his back for a post-up opportunity deep into the fourth quarter. Drawing instantly on his days back at St. John\u2019s Prep in Danover, Mass., where Connaughton was both his high school team\u2019s point guard and its most reliable big man, the Bucks player sensed where Galloway was defensively and deftly put up an 8-foot hook shot that made it 117-97, Milwaukee comfortably on its way to a 2-0 advantage in its first-round playoff series against the Pistons. \u201cHe covers a lot of ground,\u201d Bucks coach Mike Budenholzer said. \u201cHe gets some great contests even when he doesn\u2019t block shots. He bothers shooters, and he\u2019s really great at chasing and staying with guys. He mixes in making some threes and he\u2019s going to the basket and finishing. He and I were laughing that he\u2019s turned into our best power forward with a jump hook.\u201d Why not? Connaughton also is the Bucks\u2019 best hard-throwing righthanded major league baseball prospect. That\u2019s real, made official when the Baltimore Orioles made the native of Arlington, Mass., the 121st pick in the 2014 MLB draft and signed him for a $428,000 bonus. Connaughton had been fascinated with the possibility of excelling in two sports, dating back to his days rooting for both Paul Pierce and Pedro Martinez as a fan of the Celtics and Red Sox. He thrived in both basketball and baseball in high school, then sold the coaches at Notre Dame on pursuing the dream in college.  Connaughton spent the summer of 2014 in Class A, striking out 10 in 14.2 innings for the Aberdeen (Md.). Then he returned to Notre Dame to help the Irish go 32-6 and reach the \u201cElite Eight\u201d of the NCAA tournament. He averaged 12.5 points and 7.4 rebounds and made 93 of 220 (42.3 percent) from 3-point range. That spring, in his workouts for NBA teams, Connaughton showed enough to snag the No. 41 spot in the 2015 draft, a pick traded from Brooklyn to Portland that night. In three seasons with the Trail Blazers, Connaughton and his startlingly springy game averaged 3.7 points and 1.6 rebounds in 12.6 minutes. But by 2017-18, he had earned a regular rotation spot (5.4 ppg, 18.1 mpg, 82 appearances). All the while, he had friends, fans, critics and advisors whispering in his ears, one on each sport like the kid in \u201cAnimal House,\u201d only pushing the merits of baseball vs. basketball and vice versa. Connaughton fended them off to sign a two-year deal with Milwaukee last summer, and he worked through some mid-season inactivity to become a reliable call for Budenholzer now for duty at both ends. Between the best postseason game of his NBA career and the Bucks\u2019 flight to Detroit for Game 3 Saturday night, Connaughton talked with NBA.com\u2019s Steve Aschburner about the sport in his present and the other one possibly still in his future: Steve Aschburner : You and your team\u2019s other reserves have been around for this special season, contributing in ways big and small as bench players all hope to do. But to have such an impact \u2013 18 points, nine rebounds, four blocks in 31 minutes \u2013 in a playoff victory has to have been especially gratifying, doesn\u2019t it? Pat Connaughton : It\u2019s been great. But one of the things I\u2019ve tried to be better at as this year has gone on is understanding it\u2019s one game. As NBA players, so much of the game is mental, as far as confidence and playing against the best players in the world. You have to feel you belong but you also have to keep that chip on your shoulder. You need to be ready to try to do the same thing the next game and the game after that, to be as consistent as you can be. SA : Wait, that sounds almost like a coach. Coaches don\u2019t trust or can\u2019t fully enjoy the successes, even as they feel the full force of their losses. Don\u2019t you ever get to feel satisfied? PC : You have to be smart about how you do it. You pick your spots. After [Game 2], I was happy. I was satisfied with myself that night. You get a mix of texts from past friends or family or former coaches, and that\u2019s awesome, but you also hear from some people who thought you\u2019d never be able to do it. Now they think that\u2019s the best you could ever play and there will never be another game like it in your life! For me, that fuels the fire a little bit. Yeah, I played well and I\u2019m happy I was able to help my team win. But it\u2019s also kind of a backhanded compliment, when people look at it like I shouldn\u2019t be here. I should be off pitching somewhere right now and I was just \u201clucky\u201d to do it. SA : I\u2019ve noticed people are more comfortable second-guessing you because you\u2019ve had two major sports paths. If it was either the NBA or selling insurance, for example, fewer folks would be open about doubting you. But because you\u2019ve had the option to purse basketball or baseball, people feel free to offer up their opinions. PC  never going to have success at either sport because I wasn\u2019t giving my best effort at one of them. What I learned from that is, people will want to be right about what they told you more than they\u2019ll want to see you succeed at what you\u2019re trying to do. SA : I\u2019m not even going to ask you which sport you prefer, because I\u2019ve seen the inconclusive answers you give to that question. So I\u2019ll ask this instead: How do you get through four years of academics while juggling two varsity sports? PC : And Notre Dame academically isn\u2019t a walk in the park. But if it\u2019s really something you love, you\u2019ll figure out a way to get it done. I think there are a lot of people who say they want to be professional athletes, say they want to make millions of dollars, but don\u2019t necessarily like doing the hard work that goes into it. And any time there\u2019s adversity, they\u2019ll say, \u2018Well, I tried.\u2019 And go on to the next thing.  For me, it was my dream as a kid to play two sports at the highest level I possibly could. Doing that at Notre Dame just meant I had to sacrifice in ways a normal college student wouldn\u2019t have had to sacrifice. I went back to summer school every year, to make sure I got ahead of my classes. I took the hard classes in summer and was in position, if needed, to take a lighter load in the fall or spring semester. Or be in position to potentially graduate early and really prepare myself for the NBA draft combine. There were times during summer school where, on a Friday or Saturday night, I had to be in the baseball training center working on my arm strength or in the gym putting up shots. Quite frankly, it didn\u2019t feel like a \u201chave to.\u201d It was something I wanted to do. SA : Kyler Murray, the Oklahoma quarterback and Oakland Athletics prospect, has critics, as have other two-sport athletes. In your case, do you think the situation is complicated by being a white guy who\u2019s not seven feet tall and therefore not fitting into preconceived notions about NBA players? PC : Definitely. I\u2019ve tried to say it a little more politically correct. I use the phrase \u2018I didn\u2019t pass the eye test.\u2019 The eye test in the NBA is different from the eye test in baseball. If you take my build, make-up, everything into consideration, in baseball that\u2019s sought after. You don\u2019t find a 6-foot-5 pitcher with the athleticism and coordination I have all that often. It\u2019s why I was able to pick up on things very quickly when I went through a summer of minor league baseball. But in basketball, in most people\u2019s eyes, I\u2019m in the lower half or don\u2019t even make the cut for what they perceive to be an NBA player. SA : You have explained your sequencing of sports, with the NBA first and MLB perhaps later, by noting that athleticism could fade while your arm stays strong. But did you factor in the level of player \u2013 reserve vs. star \u2013 you might become in each sport? PC : I definitely have thought of that. I looked at my life from the standpoint of what I dreamed of doing when I was a kid. Obviously I\u2019ve wanted to be as successful at both sports as possible. You have to shoot for being an All-Star to have a chance to even make it in either, right? But I did think, \u201cIf I really went into baseball right now, if I dropped basketball when I was coming out of high school, I fully believe I\u2019d have had the chance to be an All-Star, to be one of the top two pitchers on a championship team.\u201d But something drew me to basketball, something drew me to having success in two sports. I\u2019m not saying there may not be a day when I play baseball \u2013 maybe in 10 years \u2013 and I might or might not reach that [All-Star] level. But it meant more to me to have success in both sports than to give up basketball before really having the opportunity. SA : How different are the sports, as you see them? PC : In baseball there\u2019s so much camaraderie and team things just from hanging around the ballpark together. But in the end, you\u2019re the one at the plate or you\u2019re the only one on the mound at that time. In basketball, everyone\u2019s involved in the play all the time. That\u2019s something I enjoy. Being a pitcher, and walking the bases loaded with no out and having to get out of the jam, it takes a mental toughness that I almost enjoy bringing on myself, to figure out how to get the outs. At the same time, to make a mistake in a basketball game and to immediately have the opportunity to run downcourt and get a block or a steal to make up for it and help your team, that\u2019s also something I enjoy. If a guy hits a home run off me, I have to wait eight batters to see him again. SA : So far in the 2019 playoffs \u2013 small sample size \u2013 you have an offensive rating of 146 and a defensive rating of 89. Those are straight-to-Springfield numbers over a career. What do you think about analytics, which got their early traction in baseball? PC : I think analytics are good for figuring out what guys do best. But I think they\u2019re more for coaches. Sometimes I don\u2019t think analytics tell the whole story. I try to not get too caught up in it. For me, it means giving the best effort that I can. And making fewer mistakes than the guy across from me. SA : Tell us something about Giannis Antetokounmpo that we don\u2019t know.  PC  has to work that hard\u2026\u201d Combined it with his talent, his size, his quickness, how much higher could his ceiling be? It started with us doing extra shooting drills and morphed into lifting extra weight. I think it\u2019s become part of who he is as a player. And he understands how having that work ethic affects the culture of your team. SA : OK, some background and personality stuff. What was the first car you owned? PC  Jeep Wrangler when I was going into my senior year of college. Got it after playing a summer in minor league baseball, with the signing bonus I got. Still have it. SA : Tell me a song you need to hear often, that means something to you? PC  \u201cHomecoming\u201d by Kanye West . It makes sure I never forget where I came from. I was once a random, little chubby kid from Arlington, Mass., who had no ties to professional sports. But I had a dream of it. Everyone who coached me, played with me, played against me had a hand in getting me to where I am today.  SA : What\u2019s your favorite place on Earth? PC  Hampton Beach, N.H. Because it was a place I finally grew up, going in the summers. And it\u2019s a place I\u2019ll always be able to go back to in the offseason. I have friends and family who have been there since the beginning. I\u2019ll be able to run around on the beach with my little cousins and act like the little kid I am at heart. SA: Your basketball coach at Notre Dame, Mike Brey, once said rather indelicately about you, \u201cHe has a horseshoe up his ass.\u201d What do you think he meant by that? PC [laughing] : I didn\u2019t see that, but it sounds like something that would be right up his alley. I think what he means by it is, I\u2019m a fortunate kid, which I definitely am. But I also think he would say I\u2019ve helped create some of that luck that a horseshoe may bring. Whether by hard work or doing the right thing or having the right people around me. \u201cIt definitely doesn\u2019t suck to be Pat Connaughton\u201d would be another one of his quotes. * * * Steve Aschburner has written about the NBA since 1980. A thing that happened today, in which choices were definitely, definitely made:"},{"id":34211,"link":"https:\/\/www.nba.com\/news\/russell-westbrooks-legacy-hinges-playoffs","date":"2019-04-20","title":"More than ever, Westbrook's legacy hinges on playoff success","content":"OKLAHOMA CITY \u2014 Russell Westbrook\u2019s greatness is an old debate he decided a few years ago by doing things no other player of his generation can do. His legacy, however, is a different matter. For all of his triple-doubles, his breathtaking highlights and a frenzied and furious playing style that would resonate in any era, it\u2019s fair to question what exactly the Oklahoma City Thunder have to show for all of his energy and effort. Westbrook is in the physical prime of a surefire Hall of Fame career but without a signature accomplishment the franchise can trumpet beyond his Kia MVP honor from the 2016-17 season. The cold truth is the Thunder haven\u2019t been able to draft off Westbrook\u2019s rage in any meaningful way at playoff time. At least not yet. They\u2019ve won a grand total of four playoff games since Kevin Durant departed these premises for Golden State via free agency in July 2016. And that includes Friday night\u2019s 120-108 victory over the Portland Trail Blazers, when Westbrook played brilliantly at times and needed every one of his 33 points, 11 assists and five rebounds to outduel Damian Lillard (32 points, 23 of which came in a 10-minute stretch of the third quarter after Westbrook hit Lillard with his infamous Rock-da-Baby celebration). Westbrook vowed after Game 2 to be better. He had to be with the Thunder in an 0-2 hole. And he was. But Westbrook and Paul George, his Kia MVP candidate teammate and the replacement for Durant on the other side of the marquee at Chesapeake Energy Arena, haven\u2019t had the postseason breakthrough either man has been searching for since they joined forces. After three games against the relentless Blazers backcourt of Lillard and C.J. McCollum, there is no indication that it\u2019s in the offing now. The fact is, the Thunder are a perplexing bunch that, despite having two of the NBA\u2019s biggest stars as headliners, sit outside of true contender status in the Western Conference as presently constituted. This is no dig at Westbrook. Far from it. He\u2019s the most competitive human being wearing an NBA uniform today, a player willing to exhaust every fiber of his being to beat you. He plays with the sort of meddle that fans of all ages can appreciate. He\u2019s universally adored in Oklahoma City, where a rabid and raucous Thunder fan base cheers his every move on the court and celebrates the way he has embedded himself into the community off the court (when others chose to chase their destiny elsewhere). He should be applauded for giving everything he has in pursuit of becoming one of the greatest players of this or any generation. But at 30 and with seemingly all the security a superstar could ask for, have we already seen the best of what a Westbrook-led team will do? Is he satisfied with chasing 50-win seasons and playoff berths and the love and respect of these fans and his teammates above all else? Can he stomach having sniffed the air of The Finals just once, that 2012 run alongside Durant and James Harden when they were all too young and naive to know that return trips to the promised land are anything but promised? Or will he push for more? Will a player who revels in doing things his way even consider an alternative operating system? Because as fun as it might be to cherish these small victories, moments like these will wind up being empty calories if this is all there is. And like so many of his generation \u2014 the 30-and-over crowd \u2014 the clock ticks on everyone in this league, all-time greats and journeymen alike. Watching it all come to an end this season for Dwyane Wade and Dirk Nowitzki, luminaries Westbrook competed against and lost out to for championships or opportunities to play for them, should serve as a salient reminder for Westbrook that he\u2019s closer to the end than the beginning. If he\u2019s not willing to look in the mirror and ask himself the hard questions, to examine his NBA existence at this stage of his career, someone else here has to be willing to force the issue and do it for him. Someone has to be willing to go there, to dig in at a time like this, and give an honest assessment of where this Thunder team is right now and whether or not they see a road to a permanent perch in the upper echelon of the Western Conference playoff chase for the foreseeable future. Because if not, if there is no light on that horizon, then perhaps it\u2019s time to dive in on the debate. No, not of Westbrook\u2019s mercurial greatness, he\u2019s already provided more than enough data in his favor; his work in crunch time Friday night being the latest exhibit. It\u2019s time to debate whether that situational, spectacular, jaw-dropping greatness, as thrilling and crowd-pleasing as it might be, can produce anything more than what we\u2019ve already seen from the Thunder and its superstar. * * * Sekou Smith is a veteran NBA reporter and NBA TV analyst. Because, as Kevin Durant said a day earlier , in full first-person swagger: \u201cI\u2019m Kevin Durant\u201d \u2014 then pausing while scanning the faces surrounding him, before continuing \u2014 \u201cy\u2019all know who I am.\u201d And now, yes, we seem to recall. Gone is the memory of a blown 31-point lead in Game 2 and multiple Durant meltdowns, replaced by a splash of normalcy and restored order in the first-round playoff series against the overmatched LA Clippers. Durant did so with stealth and controlled fury, cranking his intensity right after the tip and taking his first shot 42 seconds into the game. By halftime he had 27 points , a personal first-half best in a playoff game, doing so without making a 3-pointer. The Warriors were up 21 points in what became a runaway win \u2014 this time, minus the Clipper comeback. But you knew this was coming. \u201cNot many people can stop Kevin Durant,\u201d said Warriors forward Draymond Green, \u201cwhen he doesn\u2019t want to be stopped.\u201d That goes for Beverley, who was finally humbled and checked and didn\u2019t have the sympathy of the referees this time. There was less chatter from Durant whenever Beverley used a low center of gravity on defense. While Durant did get two first-quarter fouls , Warriors coach Steve Kerr never gave any thought to subbing him out. Kerr sensed Durant was fully under control and wouldn\u2019t do anything borderline to risk getting into foul trouble or fall for Beverley\u2019s tricks. Durant felt, perhaps with some justification, that the officiating leaned toward Beverley and cited the David vs. Goliath tale. Yet Durant took exception to any suggestion that he had to \u201cprove\u201d something in Game 3. \u201cI\u2019ve been in the league 12 years, I\u2019m 30, I don\u2019t need to show anybody nothing at this point,\u201d he said dismissively. \u201cI just play.\u201d There were some in the organization who expressed concern about Durant\u2019s persona this season and especially lately. He finished second to Green in technical fouls this season and refused to ease in the playoffs. Durant earned a pair of techs for his suspension in Game 1. In Game 3, he earned another for carrying on a spirited conversation with Clippers forward JaMychal Green far too long for referee Scott Foster\u2019s liking. That\u2019s now three techs for Durant, and assuming the Warriors are soon headed to the Western Conference semifinals, there\u2019s a lot of playoff games to be played. How will Durant\u2019s teammates and Kerr feel if Durant gets his seventh playoff technical \u2014 and the one-game suspension it draws \u2014 sometime around Game 2 of The Finals, (assuming the Warriors reach that point)? Durant vs. Beverley has referees on heightened alert, which means there will be little patience or tolerance for verbal engagement going forward. That won\u2019t hurt Beverley, not with the Clippers down 2-1 in the series and seemingly on the their way out. It only jeopardizes those who are moving on. In Game 3, though, Durant put performance over agenda and it was infectious. The Warriors fell in line and delivered a dominant win. \u201cHe\u2019s a two-time Finals MVP and coming off a poor performance,\u201d Kerr said. \u201cThat\u2019s what happens. He had a different mindset and set a tone right away.\u201d Said Clippers guard Lou Williams: \u201cHe made that \u2018I\u2019m Kevin Durant\u2019 statement with confidence. He announced himself before he came here, and we didn\u2019t respond.\u201d Warriors center Andrew Bogut said: \u201cHe was pumped. He was the KD we know.\u201d Durant was on pace for 50 points, but Kerr showed some mercy and pulled him after 29 minutes. By then, the lead was secured. Bogut blocked a shot and threw to a streaking Andre Iguodala for a dunk and a 31-point lead. It was one that the Warriors didn\u2019t surrender because improbability doesn\u2019t strike twice. The Warriors are a win away from being able to keep one eye on finishing the job by Wednesday and another eye toward the Houston Rockets. They are flexing in their first-round series with the Utah Jazz and perhaps readying for a Warriors semifinal series roughly a week from now. As a side note, Staples contained a fair number of Warriors fans Thursday, just as it did during the regular season, killing any thought of L.A. turning up for the Clippers. This may change next season (depending on the Clippers\u2019 offseason), but for now, the atmosphere should be very much the same for Game 4 on Sunday ( 3:30 ET, ABC ). That means a segment of the building will be rooting for a certain Warriors superstar to stay true to his name, reputation and track record. \u201cWell,\u201d said Clippers coach Doc Rivers, \u201che\u2019s Kevin Durant. But I already knew that.\u201d You suppose? * * * Shaun Powell has covered the NBA for more than 25 years. You can e-mail him  here  .
