"},{"id":34207,"link":"https:\/\/www.nba.com\/news\/embiid-out-redick-rises-sixers","date":"2019-04-19","title":"With Embiid out, Redick weaves way to clutch performance","content":" BROOKLYN, N.Y. \u2014 JJ Redick is his own man. For most of this season, the Philadelphia 76ers\u2019 designated shooter was a better shooter when he shared the floor with center Joel Embiid. But with Embiid missing Game 3 of the Sixers\u2019 first round series against the Brooklyn Nets on Thursday, Redick scored more points than he did in Games 1 and 2 combined. And despite the absence of their most important player, the Sixers rolled to a 131-115 victory and took a 2-1 series lead. The Sixers\u2019 offense, which ranks No. 1 in the postseason at 120.8 points scored per 100 possessions, kept rolling without Embiid, even though replacement starter Greg Monroe shot 4-for-13. In scoring 32 points or more in all four quarters, the Sixers got big games from Tobias Harris (29 points on 11-for-19 shooting, 16 rebounds) as well as Ben Simmons (31 points on 11-for-13 shooting, nine assists). But it was Redick that had the Brooklyn defense scrambling for much of the second half. After scoring 10 points on 3-for-9 shooting in the first half, Redick broke out in the third quarter to help the Sixers gain separation in what had been a close game. A 14-3 Philly run turned a three-point lead into a 14-point cushion. Redick scored nine of those 14 points during the run. First, with Jimmy Butler probing the Brooklyn defense, Redick got loose from Joe Harris for a catch in the right corner, side-stepping Harris\u2019 closeout to drain a 3-pointer and put the Sixers up eight. Two possessions later, Redick cut from the right block to the left wing, side-stepped another Harris closeout, and hit another 3-pointer to put the Sixers up eight again. And two possessions after that, he drained a more standard catch-and-shoot 3-pointer off a pin-down to push the lead to 14. After that, Redick started opening things up for his teammates. Midway through the third, D\u2019Angelo Russell took his eyes off his own man to see where Redick was going. That man, Tobias Harris, found himself open for an easy catch-and-shoot 3-pointer. With their own Harris struggling to keep up with Redick, the Nets gave the assignment to Spencer Dinwiddie. But later in the third, Dinwiddie got caught in a dribble hand-off from Simmons and Jared Dudley fouled Redick on a corner three. When the Nets went to a zone, Redick circled through it, caught the ball at the top of the floor, and hit a pull-up three that put the Sixers up 16 late in the third. The Nets got back into the game with an 8-0 run to close the period. But Redick\u2019s gravity was felt midway through the fourth. With Dinwiddie trying to deny him the ball, he flared to the corner. As Dinwiddie closed out, Joe Harris took a step away from his man, Mike Scott. Redick got the ball to Scott, who ran a quick pick-and-roll with Simmons. And Simmons got a clean roll to the basket, because Dinwiddie was still attached to Redick in the corner. Instead of Redick benefiting from Embiid\u2019s presence on the floor, it was the other Sixers benefiting from the attention paid to Redick. \u201cI thought J.J.\u2019s run there really got us all out of kilter defensively,\u201d said Nets coach Kenny Atkinson. \u201cWhen a guy gets hot like that, guys start helping and cheating, and other guys get off too. That was a big part of the game when he got going.\u201d The Nets have been top-blocking Redick all series, trying to deny his perimeter catches and keep him from getting clean runs into dribble hand-offs. So Redick has had to find different paths to the ball. That has meant running on the inside of the handoffs or flaring to whatever open space he could find. Mapping routes to his catches in Game 3 would be a fun exercise. \u201cBen and I have an amazing thing,\u201d Redick said. \u201cIt\u2019s very organic. We don\u2019t really call plays. We just get into stuff. We talk about it in the huddle if we see something that a team is doing and we feel like we can exploit it. There were three or four plays tonight when we did that.\u201d Through the first two games, both teams had kept the opponents\u2019 best shooter in check. Entering Game 3, Redick had 22 points on 9-for-19 shooting (3-for-9 on 3-pointers). Joe Harris scored 17 points on 6-for-11 shooting, going 3-for-6 from beyond the arc. On Thursday, one of the shooters broke free. The other did not. While Harris scored eight points on 2-for-7 shooting (including 0-for-4 from 3-point range), Redick finished with 26 points on 7-for-17, draining 5-of-9 attempts from beyond the arc. His nine free throw attempts were one shy of a season high. \u201cHe\u2019s elite at getting open,\u201d Atkinson said. \u201cThey did a fantastic job of screening for him. That was big. We\u2019re chasing him, he\u2019s flying off, and they\u2019re setting great screens. We didn\u2019t do a good enough job staying attached. Same game plan. We didn\u2019t change it. Just didn\u2019t execute it at a high enough level.\u201d Only two of Redick\u2019s seven field goals came off a clean catch. A less-determined shooter would think that, with the way he was being guarded, it just wasn\u2019t his series to score. But Redick hasn\u2019t stopped moving, and he certainly didn\u2019t let Embiid\u2019s absence keep him from looking for the ball. In the regular season, Redick\u2019s shooting splits (FG%, 3PT%, eFG%) relative to Embiid were: The with-Embiid and in-games-Embiid-played numbers are so similar because \u2014 when health allowed \u2014 Embiid was on the floor for 86 percent of Redick\u2019s minutes. Sixers\u2019 coach Brett Brown staggers his starters\u2019 minutes in pairs, and he has typically kept Redick and Embiid together, because of how tough it is to guard their two-man game. Asked about his poor shooting with Embiid off the floor this season, Redick brushed it off. \u201cI believe in sample size,\u201d he said. \u201cSmall sample size this year.\u201d Not really. Redick took 360 shots (222 of them from 3-point range) with Embiid off the floor. But a professional shooter is trained to forget the last failure. And Redick knows that he\u2019s had success without Embiid in the past. \u201cI think the best basketball of my career,\u201d he said, \u201cwas probably last year when we went on the winning streak.\u201d Redick averaged 19.0 points on an effective field goal percentage of 59.9 percent over the Sixers\u2019 16-game winning streak to close the 2017-18 regular season. With Embiid missing the last eight games of that run, Redick averaged 22.4 on an effective field goal percentage of 64.5 percent. And over the course of last season, Redick shot better with Embiid off the floor than he did with Embiid off the floor. The number of shots he took with Embiid off the floor in \u201917-18? 371 . So sure, this year was a (slightly) small(er) sample size. The Sixers still need Embiid to compete with the best teams in the East. But with or without him, they have both the opportunity and the ability to take complete control of this series in Game 4 on Saturday (3 ET, TNT). * * * John Schuhmann is a senior stats analyst for NBA.com. But he always seems to happen here. It\u2019s a spring tradition, the San Antonio Spurs in the NBA playoffs \u2014 22 straight seasons and counting \u2014 with a fresh-faced prospect from the incubator ready for a star turn. Tony Parker, Manu Ginobili and Kawhi Leonard all traveled this path at some point over the past two decades. In the absence of a future Hall of Famer or franchise icon to lean on, the Spurs have turned to their break-the-glass point guard. The second-year man starred against his hometown team on the biggest stage Thursday night at AT&T Center, making play after play to lead the Spurs to a 118-108 Game 3 victory against the Denver Nuggets. The seventh-seeded Spurs lead the series 2-1 thanks in large part to stellar work from the 29th pick of the 2017 Draft. \u201cHe was obviously spectacular on both ends of the floor,\u201d Spurs coach Gregg Popovich said of White, who shredded the Nuggets for a career-high 36 points (on 15-for-21 shooting), five rebounds, five assists, three steals and a block. \u201cI don\u2019t know what else to say.\u201d There\u2019s no need, Pop. White\u2019s actions did all the talking. After getting burned by Nuggets guard Jamal Murray\u2019s 21 points in the fourth quarter of a Game 2 loss in Denver, White countered with a virtuoso effort of his own. He had 26 points at halftime and flattened the Nuggets\u2019 defense with his dribble penetration to set the tone for a monster night. \u201cI felt like a lot of those were on me and I had to step up and make him work today,\u201d White said of his battle within the game against Murray. \u201cI was just trying to be aggressive and trying to get to the paint. I know a lot times they try to bluff and get back, so I just tried to finish strong when I was driving to the paint and make the right play.\u201d While Murray entered the league with all of the hype that accompanies a lottery pick from a blue blood program [Kentucky], White was a lightly recruited combo guard who started out at Division II Colorado-Colorado Springs. He finished his college days at the University of Colorado and caught the eye of the Spurs\u2019 scouting staff. The growth and development that ensued, including extended time in the NBA G League during his rookie season, is paying dividends now. White\u2019s 36 points were the most by a Spur in the playoffs since Leonard\u2019s 36 in April 2017. And he\u2019s the first Spur to finish with 35 or more points and five or more assists and rebounds in a playoff game since Parker did it in April of 2008. \u201cIt was awesome, especially defensively,\u201d said Spurs All-Star DeMar DeRozan, who burned the Nuggets for 25 points, five assists, four rebounds and three steals. \u201cHe took on a challenge from last game versus Murray, got us going and we fed off him. He led us offensively and defensively.\u201d From wicked open-court defense on Murray to a slick, no-look pocket pass to Jakob Poeltl for a layup to the presence of mind to know when to attack and when to facilitate, White played like someone who had been in the moment before. While these moments could overwhelm a 10-year veteran, none of it seemed to shake White. He was the best player on the floor in just his third playoff start. \u201cWe couldn\u2019t stop him,\u201d said Nuggets coach Mike Malone. \u201cI mean, they had 62 points in the paint. We gave up 16 blow-by, 1-on-1 containment, not game planning or anything. It was just the ability to guard 1-on-1, or the lack thereof. He was impressive. From jump street, he attacked, he got wherever he wanted and he finished. He was into us defensively. That was a hell of a performance from a young player.\u201d It was a performance that might not have been possible were it not for the torn ACL Dejounte Murray suffered in the preseason , prompting Popovich to experiment with White as his replacement. White doesn\u2019t get his chance to play hero otherwise. Well, that late Game 2 blitz from Murray certainly added some incentive to White\u2019s first playoff start before the Spurs faithful, who even showered him with a smattering of MVP chants late in the final minutes. \u201cThere\u2019s no doubt in my mind that Derrick White the last couple of days has been reminded about Jamal Murray\u2019s fourth-quarter performance,\u201d Malone said. \u201cDerrick White came out like he hadn\u2019t eaten in two days. He came out hungry, he came out pissed off and he sent a very loud and clear message. \u201cNow I\u2019m anxious to see our guys. How do we respond to that? Where is your pride? Where is your toughness? Where is your grit? So Game 4 is going to be really like a great insight into who we are as a team, especially our young players, especially Jamal Murray.\u201d White refused to play up the potential duel with Murray for Saturday\u2019s Game 4 . But he was caught up in the swirl of emotions Thursday night, pointing to his bicep and engaging the crowd. \u201cI mean, every now and then I get like that,\u201d the soft-spoken White said. \u201cIt\u2019s not really my thing. But just the emotion of the game \u2026 I think I just had a chip on my shoulder. This is the way I\u2019ve played since I was young. And I was just trying to go out there and compete and have fun. \u201cA lot of things have gone crazy. I am extremely blessed to be in this situation. I am not taking anything for granted, but just trying to stay in the moment.\u201d * * * Sekou Smith is a veteran NBA reporter and NBA TV analyst.
